Temple Administration and two important religious functionaries 299 Sculptures in the Hill-Shrine 305 Appendix-I 311 Appendix-II 319 PREFACE We have great pleasure to publish The Hill shrine of Vengadam’ as the 11th Publication of Sri Vikhanas Trust, with the divine blessings of lord Sri Venkateswara. The presiding deity of the world famous Tirumala Hill shrine is Sri Venkateswara, lord Vishnu’s direct manifestation eulogized by sages, ascetics, kings, emperors, chieftains as also praised by innumerable devotees. He was popularly called as GOVINDA", as he fulfills all the wishes of the devotees. This popular book was written by famous scholar Vidyalankara, Sastra chudamani, Sangeeta kalaratna Prof. S.K.Ramachandra Rao. The book elaborates about the various details of the main deity, discuss the inscriptions of the temple, well researched and explained by various historians, the book also has separate sections focussed on worship method Sri Vaikhanasa Bhagavachhastram’ followed in the daily rituals of the temple as well as the details of practices, customs and traditions followed during important festivals and mythology of the shrine. This book was published by Kalpataru Reaearch Academy, Bangalore in the year 1993. The copies of first edition have sold out long back which evidences its popularity in the devotees of Lord Venkateswara. The devotees are much eager to know about the hill shrine through such authentic works. Considering this demand the author, Sri S.K.Ramachandra Rao has kindly consented to transfer the copy rights of this work to Sri Vikhanas Trust, Tirumala which is engaged in publishing the books to fulfill its aims and objectives of preserving, promting and propagating the ancient Indian culture and heritage. The book is a gem of its kind and it elucidates the art, architecture and agama of the hill shrine. The content presented in the book is the outcome of extensive research by Sriman S.K.Ramachandra Rao. He presented authentic information collected from various sources related to temple to bring out the final edition. This reprint is enriched with necessary additions at appropriate places and contains more colorful pictures. We are sure that the sincere effort of the author in compiling this great authoritative work will be appreciated by the readers.We are very grateful and obliged to Prof. S.K.Ramachandra Rao for giving us the golden opportunity of publishing this work. Sri Vikhanas Trust is committed to publish more such divine books in future, and we hope that the efforts of the trust in this direction would be patronized by all the readers who are interested to know in depth, details about the Holy temple of Lord Sri Venkateswara, Tirumala. My sincere thanks to the members of Sri Vikhanas Trust, comprising, the Chairman, SrimanA.S.Narayana Deekshitulu. Hereditary Priest of Tirumala Temple. Treasurer, Sriman D. Srinivasa Deekshitulu, Senior Analyst, TTD and Secretary, SVDSV Sabha, Tirumala. Trustee, Sriman D. Raghava Deekshitulu, Tirupati. Trustee, Sriman A. Anantasayana Deekshitulu, Tirupati, Hereditary Priest of Tirumala Temple, for their continued support and encouragement for this project. My special thanks are due to Sriman Archakam Ramakrishna Deekshitulu, Hereditary Priest of Tirumala Hill shrine, for his untiring efforts in bringing out this book with more colorful pictures. Thank you for your continued support. Tirumala Vikruti Ugadi 16-03-2010 || Om Namo Venkatesaya || G. Prabhakara Charyulu Secretary Sri Vikhanas Trust INTRODUCTION When I finished the manuscript of this book, I felt relieved and fulfilled. I could truly say ‘‡§Ö‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Æ‡•á ‡§∏‡§´‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‘Today my life has been fulfilled, and I have indeed lived well’ For this was on my mind for over fifty years. Even when, as a boy, I visited this hill-shrine with my parents and grandparents, I was excited by the idyllic surroundings (which alas has disappeared in recent years) of the shrine, the majestic peaks, the deep gorges and the dense jungle through which one had to journey to reach the shrine. The god of the hills is my tutellary deity, and it has been a custom in our family for several generations now to visit the hill-shrine as frequently as possible, at least once a year. We go to no other place of pilgrimage, and in our puja-room it is only this hill-god who is worshipped. I could say, with Nammalvar, that here is the god whom my parents, their parents, their parents and all their ancestors have worshipped. 66 tiru venga·∏çattu elil-kol sodi yendai tandai tandaikku" “endai tandai tandai tandai tandaikku mundai……..’ During my annual visits as a young man I have roamed about three of the seven hills, visited the several shrines associated with the water-falls and streams on the hill, and have gone round all the apartments and enclosures of the hill-shrine. I also had occasion to study the inscriptions that abound here; I have looked at the temple’s art, and architecture rather closely. In art and architecture, it cannot of course compare favourably with most of the South Indian temples. As a student of art I found little here that was really interesting. But the history of the temples over the centuries, as revealed by the inscriptions as well as by the structures, fascinated me. The enduring hold of this shrine over the imagination and peity of the people from different parts of the country, and for great many generations, filled me with wonder. I have visited the shrine several times when travel to the shrine was rather hazardous, when facilities that we are now used to were utterly lacking, and when the sole attraction was the god of the hills that stands in the sanctum, towering over all the hills. Even then, crowds of pilgrims and devotees thronged the hill all through the year, excepting in the rainy season. I was struck by the deep devotion and single-minded zeal that were all too evident in these pilgrims, who cared for nothing else, and they did hardly anything else on the hills; especially the rural folk and tribals who climbed the hills almost mechanically went straight to the shrine as if nothing else mattered, and returned home as soon as they had a glimpse of the god of their dreams. What a contrast the current picture presents! Whatever else I was doing in my professional career, this was always at the back of my mind: to reconstruct the history of this hill-shrine and to present to myself a complete account of the worship that went on there. About fifteen years ago I did write a book in Kannada, giving as many details as I could get from my own visits and my study of the inscriptions. I wove a neat and meaningful fabric within the limits of historical accuracy. The book was no doubt well-received, and it even bagged the coveted Sahitya Academy award. But somehow I was left dissatisfied: I had not discussed the architectural unfoldment of the shrine; and had at that time very few photographs with me to illustrate the peculiarities of the apartments, the sculptures, the icons, and the festivals. I had also not unravelled many a riddle concerning the structures here. I was, however, sure that I would be able to do a more satisfying job sometime later, and that I would procure all the necessary materials to present the hill-shrine in a much clearer light. My faith in the hill-god left no room for despair. And sure enough, the pieces began falling into their places, doubts were cleared, the dark niches were illumined, and the missing links were gathered. The hill-god found me friends and favourable circumstances. The first and foremost among the gifts of the hill-god to me in the endeavour was Divi Vikhanasacharyulu (D.V. Chary), the retired Executive Engineer of the Tirumala-Tirupati Devasthanams. He was associated with the hill-shrine for more than forty years, and is intimately acquainted with every inch of the temple as well as the hill on which it stands. He is an extraordinarily intelligent, energetic, honest and efficient man. He had painstakingly collected all documents, reports, measurements, maps publications and photographs pertaining to this temple and its environs all of which he put in my possession with a readiness that was amazing, and without my having to ask for them! It was indeed a treasure for me. He was in charge of most of the alterations that have been effected in the temple in recent years, and he spent hours telling me all about them. All the photographs appearing in this book (excepting about twenty) are his contributions, as also the source-materials that have been used here. Next in the list of gifts is Archakam Srinivasa Narayana Dikshitulu of the Paidipalli family (one of the four families hereditarily entrusted with the worship of the hill-god). He made available to me several Agama texts and arranged for my observation of the icons in the sanctum and of the details of the main shrine. Himself a poet, musician, and artist, he impressed me as a devout, sincere and earnest person, a friendly and intense soul. The hours I have spent with him were most precious experiences for me. He filled my heart with his songs and with the narration of his experiences which bordered on the occult. He introduced me to the head-priest of the temple, Madambakam Srinivasa Bhattacharya, who proved a veritable mine of practical and relevant information not only about the Agamic worship in the hill-shrine, but about the hill-shrine itself. Having spent long years in the worship of the hill-god, year after year, he was probably the best authority I could dream of encountering for discussing the discrepancies between the Agamic prescriptions and the actual practices in the temple. He has taken very kindly to me, and his explanations are always marked by wisdom wit, and frankness. I consider it my good fortune that I got to know him. The friend who made my visits to the hill-shrine not only pleasant but altogether worthwhile, who is truly a selfless benefactor in various ways, and whose knowledge of the men and matters in the temple-town is remarkable, is Varanasi Chenchaiah. Should I express my gratitude formally to these gentlemen who have become my personal and valued friends? I would sound artificial and words would surely be inadequate. As Kalidasa has put it, ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§É ‡§ï‡§∞‡§£‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Ø‡§É There is a reason why I undertook to write this book. Tirumala is an important religious centre which has always attracted large crowds of pilgrims from far and near. In recent years, with the modern modes of transport and conveniences provided by the Temple administrators, the pilgrim population has increased enormously: on the average about five to six lakhs every year. The Defence Institute of Work study of Mussoorie conducted a survey in this regard in 1976, and found that there were about five hundred people arriving at the shrine every hour, and that about 850 people were passing in front of the sanctum in each of the hours ‚Ä¢ allowed for sarva-dar≈õana. The study showed that the minimum number of pilgrims that wait for the vision of the god in the sanctum is 7,569 per day, and the maximum (on Sundays, holidays and festive days) is 26,785. The actual time available for each pilgrim to stand in front of the sanctum would therefore be between two and four seconds; walking in the it would take him about eight minutes to move from the Golden Doorway (Bangaru-vakili) to the viewing point inside the front porch of the sanctum called Sayana-mantapa! And he would have waited from three to thirteen hours in the Queue Complex (depending upon the season, time of the day and day of the week), to reach the Golden Doorway!!! queue, The Queue, however, is a necessary evil here. Without this, there would be stampede all the time, and no one would be able to get anywhere near the main shrine at all. The TTD authorities must surely be thanked for the excellent queue arrangements that they have made, and for the conveniences that they have made possible. But the fact remains that every pilgrim becomes a virtual prisoner in the queue passages, and his movement along the queue lines precludes his having a look at the structural details of the temple. His movements become exceedingly restricted, and he can possibly have no idea whatever of the’ art and architecture of the temple. Not that most pilgrims would be interested in this aspect of the hill-shrine; but for those that happen to be inclined that way, the queue complex presents a handicap. It is for these few that my book has been planned. I have attempted to provide here as graphic a description as possible of the various structural details of the temple, as many particulars as were available to me of the practices prevalent in the temple. The purpose is to present an integrated picture of the shrine, the deity and the worship. It would not only be artificial but meaningless to divide them into so many disparate studies. I have taken care to consider all or most of the details within the Agama framework, which is undeniably the most relevant one. And in this sense, this book may claim to be the first of such attempts. But I do hope that other and better studies would follow. I have also taken as my prop an unusual but altogether interesting poem in Sanskrit called Sri-venkatesa-seva-krama. It is long poem of 122 short two-line verses, taking the pious pilgrim to the hill-shrine in hand, and conducting him right up to the sanctum, showing him all the spots of significance en route. The poem provides an excellent pen-picture of the hill as well as the shrine on top of it. It also describes vividly the main deity in the sanctum. I have for years been intrigued, and also fascinated, by the iconography of the so-called Venkatesa (or Srinivasa) form of Vishnu, which is the form in which we find the hill-god. It is not one of the usual or classical forms of Vishnu that we come across in the Agama texts or Silpa-sastra manuals. It does not figure in the lists of four (chatur-murti), five (pa√±cha-vyuha), ten (dasavatara), twelve (dvadasa-rupa), twenty-four (chaturvinsati-rupa) or thirty-eight (ashta-trimsad-vibhava-deva) forms of Vishnu which are wellknown in Vaishnava iconography. Legends or episodes connected with the assumption of this particular form (of Venkatesa) are not available in any early texts (like Bhagavata or Sattvata-samhita). There is, of course, the popular tale recounting Bhrgu’s curse, Vishnu’s separation from Lakshmi, Chola-raja’s discovery of the human form of Vishnu and Akasa-raja’s bestowal of Padmavati (incarnation of Lakshmi) on Venkatesa (the form of Vishnu) to be found in the Puranas. Even as a story it is a poor one: it abounds in absurdities and wild flights of fancy. It is of recent origin, and the Mahants who administered the temple were mainly responsible for its popularity. Whatever the relevance of the tale to the appearance of the hill-god here, it does not in any way explain the iconographic peculiarities of this god. It has been one of the most precious good fortunes in my life to have been privileged to observe several times the magnificent icon in the sanctum, bereft of all the glittering ornaments, flower-decorations and gilt coverings. Words fail to describe my feelings whenever I have stood in front of this icon, on Friday mornings, bewitched by its beauty, charmed by its grace, and overwhelmed by its majesty. It is truly a remarkable work of art, perfect in every detail, and most compelling in its appeal. Its spiritual attraction is too well-known to need any mention of it by me. Whether the icon is self-manifest or was fashioned by an inspired genius, there is no doubt whatsoever that “thousand eyes would not be enough to look at this god”, as a poet spoke of it: And one who stands ‡§®‡§æ‡§•‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§®‡§æ‡§ö‡§≤‡§§‡§ü‡•á ‡§®‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡§æ‡§Æ before the sanctum and obtains even a fleeting vision of the icon within, would feel fulfilled and ejaculate without words what occurred to the author of Markandeya-purana (4,5) ‡§Ö‡§π‡•ã ‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§π‡•ã ‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§π‡•ã ‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§π‡•ã ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡•á‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•É‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø ‡§∏‡§æ‡§Ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ There was a time when I was interested in the controversy concerning the identification of the icon as Vishnu, Siva, Sakti, Skanda, Bhairava or Virabhadra. I got out of it when my father chided me for my interest in such controversies which were unseemly, irrelevant, purposeless and mean-minded. Later, 1 was duly impressed with what I read in Varaha-purana (1, 16, 6-10) about this god who dwells on the sacred hill of Vengadam: “Who indeed can fathom your form as it really is? Sometimes myriad-headed, myriad-eyed and myriad footed; sometimes with but two arms, and at times four-armed; sometimes devoid of all limbs and subtly pervasive like sky. At times, you become readily known to our minds, and at other times perceived only in deep contemplation. For some, you are full of forms, shapes, qualities and aspects; and for others, totally devoid of any identifiable form or attribute. Some realize you as pure awareness, and some only as pure existence. However, what we prefer is the view that you have a magnificent and bewitching form, charming our hearts and bringing hope and succour to our lives. It comes well within our inner experience, and its authenticity as beyound doubt” ‡§ì‡§Ç ‡§®‡§Æ‡•ã ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡•á‡§∂‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§≤‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§ö‡•§ ‡§∏‡§ø‡§Ç‡§π‡§æ‡§ö‡§≤‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡§£‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§ö || ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§®‡•ç‡§ï‡•ã‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡•§ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§É ‡§∏‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ ‡§ï‡§¶‡§æ‡§ö‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§≠‡•Å‡§ú‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Å ‡§ï‡§¶‡§æ‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡•Å‡§ú‡§É ‡•§ ‡§ï‡§¶‡§æ‡§ö‡§ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§ö‡§§‡§µ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§ï‡§ø‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§ö‡•ç‡§ö‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•• ‡§Ü‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§Æ‡§ø‡§µ ‡§§‡•á ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§¶‡§æ‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡§ó‡•ã‡§ö‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§ï‡§¶‡§æ‡§ö‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§ï‡§≤‡•ç‡§™‡•á‡§® ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç ‡•• ‡§Ü‡§π‡•Å‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã‡§ö‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§Ç ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§ï‡§ø‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ ‡§ï‡•á‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•Å ‡§∏‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ ‡§§‡•Å ‡§ï‡•á‡§ö‡§® ‡•§ ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§Ø‡§µ‡§∏‡•å‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§ø‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§ø‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§µ‡§¶‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡•á‡§ö‡§ø‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ï‡§Æ‡§æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§§‡§Æ‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§π‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§Ç ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§®‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ú‡§ó‡§¶‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ I am inclined to believe, after years of study, that the hill-god was originally a folk-deity, a god worshipped by forest-tribes and hunters, and that the shrine is of very great antiquity. All available evidences point to the conclusion that the icon was earlier than the temple. For a great length of time, it must have 2 been a free-standing figure without a roof over it, and the first temple was probably built in the fourth or fifth century after Christ. The celebrity of the icon dates back to the first century at least. Although at first glance, the image seems to have early Pallava features, on closer scrutiny (which is by no means easy to accomplish in recent years, as every one who has been in the hill-shrine knows) it is characteristically pre-Pallava. In fact, the details of the icon (in so far as I was able to note, and as could be guessed from the descriptions left by the early Alvars), suggest a period for the icon which is long before iconometric and iconographic stylization, and very much anterior to the rise of Saiva and Vaishnava cults as distinct religious ideologies. It is natural, therefore, that the figure does not conform with Agamic prescriptions regarding proportions, ornamentations, hand-gestures and postural orientations. On the other hand, the celebrity of the icon must have influenced the Agama ideology in several particulars, and also the early Pallava sculptural representations. In any case, here is an account of the hill-shrine which seeks to present a comprehensive picture. I hasten to add that it is not in the nature of an academic exercise, nor in that of a research monograph. I have been a devotee of this hill-god all my life, and it is therefore that the feelings of devotion are uppermost in my mind; this is obviously the undercurrent in the whole volume. Writing this book has been for me an act of devotion. And when I have completed it, there is reason to feel relieved and fulfilled. But I am aware that it was made possible only by the will and mercy of the hill-god himself. I could not even dream of the extremely favourable opportunities that were in fact provided for me to make extensive and relevant observations, and to collect without effort all the material required for completing this work. It was not even in my wildest imagination that I ever hoped that the turn of events would throughout and consistently be in my favour, as it in fact became. I can only ascribe it to providential arrangement. How can I, then, not say that it is the hand of the hill-god that wrote, and that I was but a pen that it held? ‡§ß‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•Å‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡•Ä‡§§‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø ‡§ö ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§µ‡§®‡•ç S.K. RAMACHANDRA RAO m The Hill-God (Author’s Sketch on a Fridand CHAPTER ONE The Hill-Shrine (1) It is remarkable that a small temple to a little-known god, surrounded by high peaks and dense jungles, infested with wild animals, became one of the most popular pilgrim-centres in the country. Its celebrity has a great antiquity. The author of Tolkappiyam, an ancient Tamil classic of the first century before Christ, was acquainted with this mountain; he gives its name as Vengadam, which name has survived to our own day. AhanƒÅn≈´ru, another early Tamil classic composed in the latter half of the first century after Christ, describes vividly the festivals that were once or twice a year conducted on this hill, which attracted throngs of people from all the neighbouring tracts. The hill is here named after the chieftain Pulli (‘pulli-kunram’) of the Kalvar hill-tribes. The peripetatic Vaishnava saintpoets, the Alvars, who lived between the third and the seventh centuries, have sung of this hill-shrine in ecstatic fervour. They have described the hills The Hill-Shrine as difficult of ascent and dangerous with huge elephants and wild boars roaming about, and leopards and lions lurking. They have also recorded that people (men and women) gathered there in large numbers to worship the god of the hills. The songs of the Alvars as well as the early inscriptions suggest that there was no permanent settlement of any sort on top of the hill, although the slopes were inhabited by wild tribes (like the kalvars, koravas, kurumbas and the yanadis). It is also indicated that there sprang up a proxy temple at the foot of the hills around the seventh century for the convenience of the devotees who did not dare to undertake the arduous journey up the hills. However, the fascination of the hill-shrine was so great that this proxy-temple on the plains was soon wound up. Devotees preferred the hill-shrine, however difficult the ascent and dangerous the journey! By about the eighth century, the sanctity of the hill-shrine had already appealed to the imagination of monarchs, ministers, military generals, 1a merchants as well as the common folk from all neighbouring tracts. Periodical visits to the hill-shrine became regular feature in the area around the Eastern ghats (of which the sacred hill is a part), although the terrors of the wilderness and the risks of being robbed on the way continued, to make the pilgrims uneasy and wary. The hill-shrine also became increasingly affluent with royal patronage and numerous endowments, grants and gifts, although the place continued to be uninhabited except for a few hours during daytime. There are inscriptions available from eighth century down to the nineteenth which give us some idea of how the fortune of the hill-shrine was ever on the ascending note all through the centuries. Even when the shrine was wealthy and resplendent with a fairly extensive temple-complex and the tower above the sanctum gold-plated, it still did not have a township worth its name, and the approach to the hill was still full of trouble and risk. There was forest beyond the four streets immediately around the temple and only the priests, guards and other officials of the temple resided in the village. The conditions prevailing on the hill-top around 1800 A.D., soon after the East India Company gained control over the revenues from the temple (in 1748), documented in the Meckenzie Collections: Manuscript Translations and Reports, (Vol. XVI 48, item 6, p 449 preserved in Fort St. George, Madras) “The streets which surround the Pagoda on the mountain are inhabited, wild hogs, daily at present, come into the place and remain indisturbed by the inhabitants passing and repassing through all the streets, never attempting to bite anyone, unless they are attacked and troubled,and in that case the wild hog will tear the offender to pieces; otherwise they never disturb any person walking alone. Snakes also abound and frequently come in sight but never bite. “In the woods of this mountain, a lakh yanadies collect honey, beeswax, chendana (or Red-wood), fruits and roots by great exertions; these they dispose of by sale and also medicinal roots and other rare things by which they maintain themselves, residing in these woods, (caves) which no one ever enters”. The above extract supports the rather paradoxical conjecture that the area beyond the four streets round the temple was extremely dangerous even at the beginning of the modern period. There is also a suggestion in the above manuscript that there was the everpresent danger of one being lost in the woods; instances are also provided of members of the parties of pilgrims perishing during their journey up the hills. This was despite the celebrity, popularity and glory of the temple for several centuries before this period. 2 The Hill-Shrine A The British District Collector writing in the Asian Journal of May-August, 1831 A.D. describes not only the temple but the worship as it was conducted then. He records that “the annual net proceeds from this source” (viz. offerings by the people) was 1,02,000 rupees in 1820, and “one lakh forty two thousand and odd” in 1822. He, however, made bold to predict that “the revenues of Tripetty are on a gradual decline and will die in the lapse of years a natural death. Some of the most celebrated temples in the country are worse off. But there are still, alas, many more strong holds of the devil.” The poor officer with an understandable missionary zeal of invoking fury on the heathen, would find, were he to return now to the hill-temple, that the ‘devil’ has after all won the battle; the revenues of the temple, far from dying a natural death as he predicted, have multiplied marvelously and in terms of wealth; this shrine now “surpassed” the Vatican among the religious institutions of the world. This enlightened Britisher who “on my road to Tripetty we passed several groups of these besoted heathens, and it made the road quite lively, whilst strange anomaly, a slight reflexion excited mournful feelings”, would now be stricken dumb looking at the vast crowds of people flocking at this shrine in their thousands every day of the year; he would no doubt have more of the “mournful feelings”! The Hill-Shrine (2) The precise origins of the hill-shrine cannot satisfactorily be ascertained. But it is certain that it was an early expression of the ancient cult of deification and worship of mountains, which was almost universally practised. The Babylonian gods (like Entil) abided on hills. The Phrygian Cybele, “the mother of the mountains” stood, veiled in glory, on a mountain-top. The devotees of Zeus visualized the great god holding court on the mount Olympus. The Celtic god Poeninus was lord of the Penine Alps. The Mexican Tlabe had his home on a mountain. In Persia, the mountain Alburz (or Hara Berezaiti) was honoured as the first of the mountains on earth; the sun and the stars revolved round it, and light emerged from it and returned to it. Images of gods were set upon it in ancient times. It was ‘brilliant with holiness’ and ‘glorious with sanctity’. The Japanese word ‘Kami’ meant not only deities but mountains. -The Chinese included their hills in the class of ‘beneficent gods’ (shen); they worshipped the mountain-spirits with prayers and offerings. Mountains were regarded in all ancient cultures as possessors of unusual and inordinate power. In India, the worship of mountains is not merely a practice of the past, but it is very much prevalent even to this day. Like many other details, this particular too confirms a continuity of 3 culture in our country from a distant past to our own times. From Vedic times, all through the long stretches of time, mountains have been deified, and periodical and reverential visits to them undertaken. The folk practices as well as sophisticated religious conduct have regarded mountains not only as abodes of gods and goddesses but as divinities in their own right. Legends are legion which extol the might and merit of the mountains. The tribes of the hills and forests (like the Che√±chus, Gonds, Bhils, Santals, Irulas, Kurumbas, Malaipandarams, Totos, Yanadis, Birhors etc,) adore the mountains, and look upon them as alive and holy, persistent and benign presences, and protectors of the folk who live in their vicinity. The great gods of classical Hinduism are invariably associated with hills, valleys, streams and jungles. The Himalayas, the Vindhyas, the Sahyadri ranges, the Malayan mountain, the ≈öri-saila group, Arunachala and Nilachala are some of the better known among the thousands of hill-shrines in the country. The puranas celebrate their greatness and relate tales of their glory. The mountain with its great height, immensity and mighty presence naturally evokes in the sensitive mind the sense of the mysterious. The natural dangers in the fastness of the forests that cover the mountain, the strange and frightening sounds produced by the elements of nature as well as by the wild animals, the 4 persistent veil of mist and the canopy of clouds conspire to render the mountain at once wierd and fascinating. While located on earth, it pro vides a haven on haven on its top for the denizens of the celestial realms. The early religious consciousness is inclined to endow holiness on the mountain itself; a shrine on top of it is but incidental. The Indian temple is in fact built in the image of a mountain. Many are the inscriptions where a temple is likened to a great hill. The hills that are now collectively called Tirumala (or in Tamil Tirumalai, Sri-giri in Sanskrit, meaning the ‘great hills’) form the northern part of the Eastern ghats, which constitute a long range with numerous passes from the plains (Kollur in Chandragiri taluk, Mogili in Chittoor taluk and Sainagunta being the principal passes) in Chittoor district of Andhra Pradesh. Situated on 13¬∞41’ North latitude and 79¬∞21’ East longitude, the seven hills are connected with the Eastern Ghats through Sesha chalam hills in the Cuddapah district, Nallamalai ranges in the Karnool district and Nagari hills of Chittoor district. They rise to a maximum height of 3,622 feet above sea level, and are traditionally described as a collection of seven peaks or ridges (hence the expression Sapta-giri), called in the puranas by different names: Seshachala, Vedachala, Garudachala, A√±janachala, V·πõshabhachala, Narayana chala and Venkatachala. The total extent of the sacred hills is one The Hill-Shrine hundred square miles, and the jungle path across the length of the range of seven hills would be about twelve miles. Of the seven peaks, the first three of them are low, almost flat, while two others are separated by a deep gorge (known as Auvachari- kona). The entire range of the hills is visualized as the divine dragon Adisesha, The head being Tirumalai (Venkatesvara), the tail being Sri-saila (Mallikarjuna, about 180 miles to the north of the foot-hills of Tirumalai), and the middle portion Ahobila (Narasimha, about 120 miles to the north of the Tirumalai foot -hills). The latter two sacred spots are in the Nallamalai ranges in Karnool district in Andhra Pradesh. The hill range in Chittoor district, however, is known as Seshachala. We read in Bhavishyottara (2, 4346): ‡§¨‡•à‡§ï‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§† ‡§Æ‡§π‡§∂‡§æ ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡§£‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ß ‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡§∞‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§ó‡§§‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡§É‡•§‡•§ ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ú‡§®‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§£: ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§∂‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ú‡§®‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§§‡§É‡•§ ‡§µ‡§¶‡§®‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü ‡§ó‡§ø‡§∞‡§ø‡§É ‡§®‡•É‡§Æ‡§ø‡§π‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§É ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∂‡•à‡§≤‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§ó‡§ø‡§∞‡§ø‡§É ‡•§‡•§ The hill on which the shrine is situated is about 2,800 feet above mean sea level The standard Benchmark noted on a stone slab in the left hand corner of the entrance to the temple on the hill, (probably during the late 1800s) gives 2916.70 feet above mean sea level as the height of the hill-top stands. It is, on which the temple however, not the highest hill. In fact, this hill is surrounded by taller peaks. To the north of the temple, the hill on which a trigonometrical station in recent years has appeared is 3,426 feet in height; near Jabala-tirtha the height is 3.177 feet. To the east, the hills rise to heights between 2,250 and 2,750 feet. To the south, the hill stands 2,920 feet in height. On the south-west the peak known as Narayana-giri rises to a height of 3,620 feet, about a mile away from the temple along the Ghat road. The land to the west of the hill is 1,800 feet above sea level The early references to the god on the hills was that he stood on the highest peak (in Tamil, ‘uchchiyil ninran’); but the references after 980 A.D. show a realization that actually the god stands on a low hill, surrounded by higher peaks (‘malai-kuniya ninran’, “one who stands where the hill bends”) Vadiraja-svami, who is reputed to have worshipped in this shrine for twelve years (1486-1498 A.D.) at the bidding of the Vijayanagar monarch Krishnadeva-raya, describes the shrine in his Tirtha-prabandha as located in a basin between two tall mountains. ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§µ‡•á‡§ï‡§ü‡§ó‡§ø‡§∞‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§£‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•ã‡§£‡•Ä ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§£‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Ä ‡•§ ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∞‡•á‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ó‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§ó‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§≠‡§ú‡§æ‡§Æ‡§π‡•á ‡•§‡•§ The Hill-Shrine 5 A natural rock-formation in the form of an arch (hence now known as sila-torana) to be seen near Chakra-tirtha by the side of the road that leads to the Microwave Station. Geologists have identified the rocks on the hill as Precambrian; and this arch-formation suggests an antiquity of several million years to the hill. It is said that there are only two other natural rock-arches of such great antiquity in the world. 6 The Hill-Shrine (3) F. Wilkinson, who was the acting Sub-collector of Salem, sent a note on September 10, 1873 to W.S. Whiteside, Collector of North Arcot, as required by the Board of Revenues, giving a graphic description of the hill-shrine and the approach to it. Excerpts from this note (paragraphs 3 to 7) bear reproduction. “The range of the hills on which the celebrated Tirupathi pagoda stands are situated in the north east corner of the Taluk. The ascent commences at the Adipadi-gopuram or portal which is about half a mile distant from lower Tirupathi. Beyond this gopuram none but Hindus are allowed to proceed. At the summit of the slope stands the Gali-gopuram or portal where a light is kept burning every night. The distance from here to upper Tirupathi is six miles. The road or path which passes through scrub-jungles, and is in many places very precipitious is paved with stone. There are mantapams and halting places at different intervals.” “About halfway between the Galigopuram and upper Tirupathi, there is a fine well, said to have been sunk by one of the predecessors of the mahant. Joining it is a mattam (Skt. ‘matham’) and a tope (viz. grove, Kannada ’topu’) containing fruit trees is kept up by the mahant himself.” “The village of Tirumalai or upper Tirupathi lies in a hollow, surrounded by hills on every side but the east or The Hill-Shrine south-east. In the centre is the temple with its gilded dome. Round the temple runs a broad street, along which the car is dragged. Facing the street are a large number of mattams (viz., monastic institutions) and chutrams (viz., charitable centres where free food and lodging is provided for pilgrims), belonging to different zamindars and private individuals… At the back of these mattams and chutrems are topes (viz., Topu’, groves) containing jack, orange, plantain, mango and other fruit-trees. On the south-west on the path leading to Chandragiri is a fine tank from which the topes are watered.” “Beyond the topes is jungle. There are four roads or paths from upper Tirupathi, the most frequented path is the one which leads to lower Tirupathi, passing through Gali-gopuram; another path leads to Chandragiri; a third to Balapali, and a fourth to the tirthams or water falls”. The sacred hill is traditionally approached from Tirupati, as the above note mentions. Tirupati was for long a tiny village, but now a town, on the plains overlooking the hills, at a distance of less than two miles from the foot of hills. The pathway described above would be about seven miles from Tirupati (or Lower Tirupati in the above note) to the hill-shrine (or Upper Tirupati, better known as Tirumala), and is covered in about three hours. Crossing the seven hills along this route could be delightful, enchanting, awe- 7 J 1 C ( of t View of the Hills from Tirupati inspiring and breath-taking, with fine views of cliffs and dales covered by luxuriant vegetation. Most of the journey is accomplished by easy ascents and descents over hill-slopes but there are two rather stiff climbs over steep hill-sides covered by steps: one at the commencement itself (the entrancetower known as Alipiri or Adipadi leading to it), and the other just before we enter the holy precincts on top. There is more recently a motorable road, stretching to about twelve miles, from the outskirts of Tirupati right up to the shrine. Tirupati (called Kottur after a neighbouring tract before the the days of Ramanuja), however, became cele brated only from the twelfth century. after the advent of Ramanuja (10171137 A.D). There were at least three other approaches to the hill-shrine before this period. The route from Chandragiri, another town of considerable political significance from about 990 A.D., is a much shorter one, a little more than two miles in distance from the foot of the hills known as ≈öripati-vari-mettu. But it involves a stiff climb over a steep hill. However, the pilgrims who take this route will reach the shrte from behind, which is regarded as inauspicious, whereas the route from Tirupati will take him directly to the main entrance of the shrine. Devotees prefer walking along the main street (sannidhi) facing the deity, and so the route from Tirupati —ç—Å n 3 d ex an 8 The Hill-Shrine became more popular, the additional reason being the comparative ease in the hill. The ascending present township of Chandragiri is about seven miles west of Tirupati, and about five miles away to the south of the hill. A memorandum dated 16th of March 1874 from the Collector to the Secretary of the Board of Revenues gives the distances covered by the roads from the hill-top to places below. The road leading to Tirupati was given as 9337 yards (five miles, two furlongs and ninety-seven yards); the road to Chandragiri 3920 yards (two miles, one furlong and one hundred and eighty yards); and the road to Balapalli (the third route) 14745 yards (eight miles, three furlongs and five yards). The road from Balapalli begins with the Chittoor district border. In deciding the boundaries of this sacred hill-shrine, the East India Company, which took over the control of the shrine’s revenues, instituted an inquiry around 1873 A.D. The general opinion was that the area considered holy upon this hill “begins with a tirtham named Srinivasa-pushkari≈Üi (where are some feet of the god on the rock) about two miles from Balapalli on the north-east; that the eastern boundary of Tirumalai is Kirukamadi and Mamandoor, the western Nagapatla, Gopalapuram and the Cuddapah frontier and the southern Adipadigopuram”. Broadly, however, ‘on the Balapalli side by Srinivasa pushkarini and on the east Tumburukona are the The Hill-Shrine boundaries of the hill which belongs to the god” (cf. note by F. Wilkinson). An important consideration was the location of tirthas (water-falls, rivulets or ponds), which were visited periodically by the pilgrims. “On these occasions, people bathe in the pools, the puranas are read and rice is distributed”. The note prepared by Wilkinson also noted that “all these thirthams are situated on the northern and eastern sides alone, and there are none on the south and west” (paragraphs 27 and 30). The pathways leading to these tirthas were also carefully measured in order to ascertain the extent of the territory of the hill-shrine. Although the sacred hill is at present known as Tirumala (in Tamil Tirumalai), meaning “the great hill”,
The Hill-Shrine
the traditional name which has been in vogue from a distant past is Vengadam (Sanskritized as Venkata, and in Telugu and Kannada Vengala). All the ancient Tamil classics (like Tolkappiyam and Silappadikaram), the songs of the Alvars, and the early and medieval inscriptions give only this name to the hill-shrine. The earliest inscriptional reference to the hill as Tirumala is dated 1013 A.D.; an inscription of that period speaks of “annai katti tirumalaikku” (Inscriptions of TTD, I, 19, during the reign of Rajendra Chola I). Even here, the name may be more descriptive than definitive. The Puranas also frequently make use of the Sanskritized form of Vengadam, as for instance, in Skanda-purana (3, 37. 11)

had twenty-four districts each of which was called a kottam. And a kottam had its own subdivisions called nadus. A kottam signified an important temple-town after which the entire district was named. Many of these temple-

That this hill was always considered sacred becomes evident not only from the references in ancient Tamil classics but also from the accounts in the puranas. Hence the later puranic conjectures about the probable meaning of the word seek to establish this sanctity. The word ‘Venkata’ (the Sanskrit form of Vengada) is made to suggest diminution, burning or destruction (kata) of all
(kata) of all sins (ven). Bhavishyottara, says:
‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡•Å: ‡§ï‡§ü‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§¶‡•ç‡§¶‡§æ‡§π ‡§â‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡•§

likewise, Brahma·πá·∏ça-purana (6, 44) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§¶‡§π‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ü‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡§æ‡§ö‡§≤ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç ‡•§‡•§
In Sanskrit, the expression ‘kata’ signifies showering or enveloping (‘kate

15
varshavara·πáayoh’). Trikanda-sesha, however, takes the word to mean a crematorium, buring ground (‘pa√±chavasthah katah sthƒÅnah,
sma≈õanam tu satanakam). And the expression ‘vyamhah’ is Vedic (cf. ·πögveda 10, 155,1) and means different kinds of evils and sins (‘vividhani papani’) (cf. Taittiriya-samhita: vya·πÅhah prataryagne prataran na ayuh’), The word Venkata is resolved as a corrupt form of the Prak·πõt ‘Vyamkata’ (which form is still prevalent in Marathi), meaning ‘vyamhah katayati’. Vedanta-desika suggests that the word ‘vinai’ in Tamil also means sins, and that ‘ka·∏çam’ (katam) means laying waste or destroying. Venkata, according to him, is derived from the Tamil ‘Vinai-ka·∏çam’ or Vengadam.
‘Kadam’ in Tamil means not only arid waste (cf. ‘aranguram’, comm. on Silappadikaram), but a mountain-slope (‘malaich charal,’ Pingalanthai) or shrubby jungle (‘purvani konda budarukada-ttidai’, Nartinai, 48). There is a Vedic word which means just this, and which is very close to Venkata, ‘vikata’. A hymn in Rig-veda (mandala 10, annuvaka 12 sukta 155, ascribed to Sirimbitha-bharadvaja), employs this word almost in the same context, and it is small wonder that Bhavishyottara-purana (4,21) quoted this hymn as a scriptural authority (sruti):
‡§Ö‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡§ø ‡§ï‡§æ‡§£‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ï‡§ü‡•á ‡§ó‡§ø‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ ‡§∏‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡•á
The deity (devata) of this hymn is the destroyer of ill-luck (Alakshmighna). The hymn entreats the evil (‘arayi’, adayini, miserliness, ‘kane’, kutsita sabda-kari≈Üi), which is of deformed gait or nature like the torturous pathway of a densely forested hill (padapatha of ‘vi-kate’ is vi+akate; meaning ‘vik·πõtagamane, vik·πõatangi va, Sayana), to disappear in the loneliness of the mountain (‘girim’, nirjana-prade≈õam).
There is also another explanation offered. The commentary on Tiruvaimoli (3, 36 i·∏çu) takes the word to be comprised of ‘ver’ (burning) and ‘kadam’ (debts viz., the misery of indebtedness). The latter term signifies, according to this work, the three debts with which an individual is born (debt to the gods, ‘deva-ma’, debt to the ancestors, ‘pity-ma’, and debt to the sages, ‘rshi-rna’) and to discharge himself from which he strives all his life. Vengada is the hill which helps him accomplish this purpose.

Varaha-purana (4, 31) provides altogether a different meaning ‘vem’ is seed of immortality, while ‘kata’ is immeasurable wealth; vemkata thus accommodates the benefits of ultimate release from the bonds of transmigration (moksha or nihsreyasa) and of prosperity in this life here and now (abhyudaya); ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ã ‡§Ö‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡§¨‡•Ä‡§ú‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§ï‡§ü‡§Æ‡•à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡•à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ò‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•à‡§ï‡§ü‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡§É‡•§ 16 The Hill-Shrine But Skanda-purƒÅ≈Üa (3,10) suggests that Venkata is only a corrupt form of Vaikuntha, which is Vishnu’s natural abode. There are references like ‡§µ‡•à‡§ï‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§†‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§®‡§ø ‡§∂‡•à‡§≤‡•á‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§≠‡•É‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡§æ ‡•§ ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡•ã ‡§≠‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§®‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§§‡§µ ‡•§‡•§ ‡§∏‡•Å‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∏‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§ò‡•å‡§ò‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§¶‡§Æ‡§æ‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§µ‡•à‡§ï‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§†‡§∂‡•à‡§≤‡•á ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡•§ (ibid 50,70) The names Vaikuntha and Vengada (Venkata) no doubt bear a close resemblance, and for the Sanskrit Vaikuntha, Vengada may reasonably be the prakrit form (tadbhava). Bhavishyottara (2, 42-44) describes this hill like unto Vaikuntha, verily the form assumed by Vishnu’s couch Sesha. ‡§∏‡•Å‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§ó‡§ø‡§∞‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§∏‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§É‡•§ ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡•ã ‡§∏‡•ã ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ß‡§æ‡§§‡•Å‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§ø‡§§‡§É ‡•§ ‡§µ‡•à‡§ï‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§†‡§∏‡§¶‡•ç‡§¶‡§∂‡•ã ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡§£‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§É‡•§ The celebrated Vaishnava saints, known as the Alvars (who lived between the third and the seventh centuries A.D.) have visited and described this sacred hill, which is known to them only as Vengadam. They confirm the general notion prevailing at that time that the mountain itself was held sacred and their songs indicate that the entire region, hill-ranges with peaks as well as the plains around them was one vast and thick jungle, at quite a few places impenetrable. The hill, Vengadam, according to them, abounded in huge black elephants, poisonous snakes, wild pigs, monkeys, lions and yalis which roamed about freely. The slopes were marked by tall bamboo bushes “growing upto the sky”, decay and sprout again growing to great heights. The forest on top of the hills ‘rubbed the sky’. There were numerous streams with “flowery creepers entwined on either bank”, which developed into torrential rapids due to heavy rain. There was dense fog on the hills most of the year, and the woods were cool and fragrant. There were flower-groves “with honey dripping from their branches, sucked by humming beetles and buzzing bees”; there were ponds filled with lovely lotuses. But there were also the Korava tribesmen, who drove away the elephants with bows and arrows. The early Alvars refer to their valour as well as their splendid appearance. Other tribes like Kurumbas, Irulas, Yanadis, Chenchus and Kadars are known to have resided in the Nallamalai ranges. They moved about fearlessly in the jungles and were excellent hunters. It is probable that they had their scattered settlements close to the hill-ranges. In fact, the early Yadava kings were kurumbas originally; they overpowered the more primitive hunting tribes (vedars, ventari) and founded their settlements in Narayanavanam on the banks of the The Hill-Shrine 17 Aruna river (three miles from presentday Puttur). the “They are the modern representatives of ancient Pallavas, who were once so powerful in southern India. In the seventh century the power of the Pallava kings seems to have been at its zenith, though very little trace of their greatness now remains. But soon after this, the Kongu, Chola and Chalukya chiefs succeeded in winning several victories over them, and the final overthrow of the Kurumba sovereignty was effected by the Chola king Adondai about the eighth century A.D. and the Kurumbas were scattered far and wide” (A.F. Cox., Manual of the North Arcot District, revised by H.A. Stuart, Madras, 1895 p.220) Even as late as 1804 A.D., “there were about a lakh of yanadis who collected honey and bees’ wax and sandal wood, fruits, and roots by great exertion. These they dispose of by sale and also medicinal roots and other rare things by which they maintain themselves, residing in these woods” (cf. Meckenzie Collections, Manuscripts translations and Reports, vol XVI, ITEM 6, p. 449). The Yanadis are a dark-skinned tribe of middle height. “They are regarded as possessing magical powers, and are often applied to for fortune-telling, spells and such exercises of their power by members of the lower castes’””" (A.F.COX. op. cit, p. 250) The Tamil songs of the Alvars make it clear that none but the Korava tribesfolk lived in the woods on Vengadam, but they also describe the fascinating festivals that were OCcasionally held on the sacred hill to participate in which large groups of people assembled from the neighbourhood. The most picturesque of these festivals, according to the early Alvars, was the one held on Mukkoti-dvadasi (‘parasi-na!’), when men and women with incense and flower-garlands ascended the hill. It is not clear if there was at that time a temple to the god of the hills (whom the Alvars uniformly call Venga·∏çattu-meyan or Vengadattan), but the hill itself was held in high esteem: “It annihilated the sins”. Even when the shrine became celebrated, the sanctity of the Vengadam hill did not disappear. Vedanta-desika, for instance, begins his Dayasataka (a hymn of hundred verses eulogizing the mercy of the god on the hill Vengadam) with an adoration of the hill, which is verily the form assumed by the grace of the great god, even as the flow of sugar-cane juice gets solidified as a rock of sugar: ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§™‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ó‡§ø‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§Ø‡§æ ‡•§ ‡§á‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§∏‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ ‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∂‡§∞‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ Even as late as 1803 A.D. Geo. Stratton, who was the Collector of Chittoor noted: “In such veneration is even the hill held the hill held that pilgrims 18 The Hill-Shrine resorting to the pagoda prostrate themselves to the ground first getting a sight of the range of hills connected with it” (Report to the Board of Revenue, dated 31st January 1803 ). was The hill that provided the scene for the self-manifestation of God. traditionally looked upon as the worthy offspring of the mythical mountain of gold, Sumeru, which is the hub on which the universe revolves.
