The puranas prescribe that before one thinks of ascending the hill, he must seek forgiveness from it: “It is a golden mountain, and all the gods abide in it and adore it. How can a mortal set his foot on it ?” But one has to ascend the hill to behold the its ’top. great god that dwells on Therefore, one prays the hill to forgive him and permit him to see the god; and he must walk on it gently, with humility in his heart (Skanda-purana, 9, 10-13) ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø ‡§ó‡§ø‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Å ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§æ‡§ö‡§≤ ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§™‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•á‡§µ‡§ø‡§§ ‡•§‡•§ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ω‡§™‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§ß‡•ç‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∏‡§π‡•§ ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§π‡§Ç ‡§™‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ó‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•§ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ ‡§§‡§¶‡§ò‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§ö‡•á‡§§‡§∏‡§É ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§®‡§ø ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ß‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ ‡§Æ‡•á ‡•§‡•§ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§®‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•á‡§ï‡§ü‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø ‡§®‡§ó‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§Æ | ‡§§‡§§‡•ã ‡§Æ‡•É‡§¶‡•Å‡§™‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡§æ‡§µ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡§æ‡§ö‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ The puranas describe this hill as enduring through all the aeons: in the first aeon it was called V·πõshadri (or Vrshabhachala); in the second Anjanadri, in the third Seshadri and in 19 the fourth Venkatachala (viz., Vengadam). ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•á ‡§µ‡•É‡§∑‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§µ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§§‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§®‡§æ‡§ö‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡§∞‡•á ‡§∂‡•á‡§∑ ‡§ó‡•à‡§≤‡•á‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡§≤‡•Ä ‡§µ‡•á‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§ü‡§æ‡§ö‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ó‡§≠‡•á‡§¶‡•á‡§® ‡§∂‡•à‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§π‡§ø ‡•§‡•§ (5) It is a matter of pure conjecture as to when it was that the temple appeared on the hill. As mentioned above, the ancient Tamil classics and the songs of the early Alvars do not refer to any temple having been there. Silappadik√§ram, however, speaks of the hill as Vishnu’s (‘senkan nediyon kunram’). The early Alvars also identify the god of the hill as Vishnu, whose image they saw on the hill. Their songs suggest that it was a free-standing image, exposed to sun, moon, wind and the rains. The later Alvars indicate presence of a low stone enclosure round the image, allowing the image to be seen from all sides. (e.g. Tirumalisai-alvar). The presence of a regular temple is not documented until 830-850 A.D., when a local ruler of the Bana dynasty, Vijayaditya, made arrangements for a perpetual light to temple to the hill-god (nanda-vilakku for Tiru-venga·∏çattu-peruman-adiga! 1, 3). But then it must have been a small shrine, and people must have visited it only once or twice a year, during festivals. The hill was still a thick forest, filled with dangers of all sorts, and inaccessible to all but the brave and 20 sturdy, as evident from the songs of the Alvars. It is probable that considering the long-standing and ever-increasing celebrity of the god of the hills (who has always been known simply as the ‘god of the Vengadam hill’, ‘Vengadam-udaiyan’, without any sectarian labels), a proxy temple, housing a representative image of the god of the hills, was set up at the foot of the hills, for the benefit of the devotees and for assuring daily worship. In fact, an inscription of 830 A.D. found in the Padmavati temple at the present day Tiruchanuru refers to Ula·∏•apperumanar, who was a Chola chief governing Chola-nadu under a Pallava ruler, making a gift of thirty kalanju of gold for burning a lamp in the temple that was set up at Tiruchanuru (called in the inscription Tirucchokinur, a village in Ku·∏çavur-nadu district of Tiruvenkata-kottai division). The god is called Tiru-vilan-koil peruman-adigal’ (the god of the minor shrine or provisional temple, ‘balalaya’ or ’tarunalaya’ in the Agama terminology, ‘ilan-koil in Tamil), who was deemed to be representative of the god of the Vengadam hills (’tiru-venkatattu emperuman-adigalukku elundaruvitta tiru-vilan koil-peruman adigalukku…’) The expression ‘balalaya’ (tiruvilan koil, young temple’) signifies a temporary or make-shift shrine when the main temple is being constructed or repaired It is not meant to be a regular or permanent temple. The deity The Hill-Shrine worshipped here would eventually be accommodated within the sanctum. This structure, according to Agama must be situated to the east or north east of the main shrine, and must be square in shape (e.g. Raurav√§gama, 27, 5). It may further have a pavilion (mantapa) in front of it, called ‘agramantapa’. The ’tiru-vilan-koil’ in this case was obviously in the hill-shrine, in front of the sanctum. It is probable that the hill-shrine had been in a state of disrepair, and the ‘kautuka-bera’ was housed in a temporary substitute shrine. While we get to know that such a temple existed in Tiruchanuru no traces of it have survived now; it must have fallen into disuse or must have been closed, when hill-shrine was repaired and became more easily accessible, with forests cut down and pathways made. In any case, the sanctity attached to the hill-shrine could never be transferred to any other shrine, however representative. But Tirucha nuru, which was a deva-dana village, bestowed upon the hill-shrine, continued to be the seat of administration (including the arrangement of worship in the hill-shrine) till at least 1013 A.D. An officer, who probably was a governor of a province (adhikari Korra-mangalam-udaiyan) encamped at Tirucha nuru to settle a case concerning the hill-shrine. The assembly of the the deva-dana village (tiru-mundiyam) had received twenty-three gold coins (pon) to light twenty-four lamps in the The Hill-Shrine hill-shrine, including one of camphor. But the servants of the temple were burning only two lamps; because of some difficulty in the transference of funds. The officer heard the case and ordered that the necessary money must immediately be transferred to the temple funds to enable all the twenty-four lamps to be lit. This inscription suggests that the hill-shrine was already there: the lighting of as many as twenty-four lamps would imply the presence of a sanctum and a covered circumambulatory pathway (pradakshina-patha), with a porch (mukha-mandapa) in in front of the sanctum. There is also reference to ghee and other articles of daily provision being transported to the hill-shrine from Tiruchanuru. Tirupati (Kottur) had not, at that point of time, become important enough to serve as the gateway to the hill-shrine. It rose into prominence only during the rule of the Yadava kings, and after Ramanuja arrived on the scene. We have another inscription dated 614 A.D. (TT No. 18, this is a correction of opinion that this inscription is dated 966 A.D.), assigned to the reign of the Pallava king Mahendra-varman (600630 A.D.) and found in the first prakara (north wall) of the hill-shrine, which records that Samavai(kadavan-perun-devi), daughter of a Pallava chieftain (pallavap-pergadeyar) and wife of the kadava chief Sakti-vitankan another Pallava chief, got a silver image installed in the 21Tiru-vilan-koil of the hill-shrine, for purposes of regular worship (karmarchana). This inscription will be noticed again when we discuss about the mobile icons in the shrine. But it should be mentioned here that the hill-shrine had a’ tiruvilan-koil’ of its own, quite distinct from the later (830 A.D.) one at Tiruchanuru. The location of the Tiru-vilan-koil in the present hill-shrine will be discussed later. Yet another inscription which suggests the growing popularity of the hillshrine is dated about 1000 A.D. and found in the western wall of Mukkotipradakshina of the hill-shrine (TT No. 117). Ulaga-ma-devi (Danti-saktivitanki), queen-consort of the Chola king, Raja-raja I (985-1005), provided a lamp for Tiru-venkatam-udaiyan, the god of the hill-shrine. Thus the temple was already becoming celebrated, although there is no inscriptional reference whatever to the founding of the temple. The puranas however, ascribe the origin of the temple to Rangadasa of the Pandya-country. Even before his days, it is said that a Haihaya king, Samkha by name, had excavated the image of the god from an ant-hill under a tamarind tree on this hill-top in the latter part of the first aeon (Brahma-purana, 6, 20-21) ‡§µ‡§≤‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§£‡•ã‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡•á ‡§∂‡•Å‡§≠‡§æ‡•§ ‡§∂‡§Ç‡§ñ‡•á‡§® ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§™‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§π‡§∞‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§≤‡§æ‡•§ ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á ‡§®‡§ø‡§Æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§æ ‡§π‡§ø ‡§§‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä‡§µ‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§≤‡§§: ‡•§ In course of time, a Vaikhanasa priest, Gopinatha by name, was ascending the hill everyday to worship this image (‘pritya jagama sailendram venka·π≠akhyam sva-siddhaye’); and he was joined by a sudra devotee, Rangadasa, who undertook to provide flowers for daily worship. The two of them are said to have lifted the image from under the tamarind tree to its left side and provided a hut-pavilion (’traih k·πõtva cha mandapam’) for it (ibid.30); ‡§§‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä‡§µ‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§π‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§â‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§Æ‡•á ‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§™‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡§§‡•Å‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§§‡•å‡•§ According to Varaha-purana (9, 1650), Ranga-dasa, mentioned earlier, when he was travelling in the vicinity of Narayana-puri in Tonda-mandalam, heard about the hill-shrine and went through a thick jungle to reach it. He was joined by the Vaikhanasa-priest, Gopinatha, and the two of them beheld the image of the god “poised in air, “viz. open to the elements (‘akasastha’), beneath a tamarind tree on the southern bank of the pond, now known as Svami-pushkarini. Ranga-dasa resolved to raise a flowergarden in the vicinity. Both Gopinatha and Ranga-dasa lived on the hill, levelled the ground and cleared the jungle around the tamarind tree, and built a wall of stones alround the image. Ranga-dasa raised a garden of 22 22 The Hill-Shrine flower-plants, creepers and trees. He spent seventy years in the service of the hill-god, and after he died he was reborn as Tondaman, the ruler of Narayana-vanam. According to the Puranas, it was this Tondaman who was responsible for the shrine on the hill. Son of Suvira and Nandini, he married Padma, the daughter of a Pandya king, and ruled from Narayana-pura. He discovered the image of the God “standing under a tree, with the lower part of the body hidden in an ant-hill” when he ascended the hill in the company of a hunter (nishada), who was cultivating syamaka grains in his fields on the hillslope. The purana says that the local deity, Varaha (wild boar), entered into the hunter and through him gave out to the king the direction that enclosure for a temple must be constructed (ibid, 10, 1-53). Tondaman constructed the prakara with a gateway with a gopuram on it, and arranged for regular worship of the god. The oracle had told him that the actual temple would be built by a descendent of his. But other sources (viz. BrahmapurƒÅ·πáa, 7, 55-57 and Bhavishyottara, 13, 31ff) say that Tondaman built the entire temple-complex with prakaras and two gopuras. ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§∏‡§Ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ó‡•ã‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§∏‡§Ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§ó‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§∞‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã‡§¶‡•ç‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ Two The description of the temple said to be built by Tondaman given in these two puranas (especially the latter) is derived from the temple that actually existed when this section of the puranas (Venkatachalamahatmya) came to be written (around the 15th or 16th century) of It would not be correct to dismiss the puranic account as altogether unreliable. Tondaman, for instance, is not a fictitious character. He was a historical personage. We know from other sources that this Tondaman chakravarti was Adon·∏çai (Audanda-cholan), the illegitimate son of Kulottungachola who ruled at Tanjavur (cf. A.F. Cox. North Arcot District Manual. Vol. I, p.39). He was the governor Kalahasti, and it was he who cleared away the jungles and built Tonda-mandalam in the vicinity of Kalahasti. He is also reputed to have attacked the Pallava forces. However, his date is uncertain. He appears to have been a contemporary of lan-tirayyan, the grandson of Karikala-chola, who lived about 550 A.D. The kingdom that Tondaman founded came to be known as Tondama-nadu (or Tonda-mandala), and later as Jayan-konda-chola-mandala. This area is immediately below the Vengadam hill-ranges; and the first temple on the hill is associated with his name. Bhavishyottara-pura·πáa (3, 15-16) also describes the first builder of the hill-temple as a Chola prince born to a girl of the Naga tribe. The Hill-Shrine 23 ‡§Ö‡§§‡•Ä‡§§‡•á ‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§∞‡•á ‡§ï‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§ö‡•ã‡§≤‡§æ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§®‡•É‡§™‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡•ã ‡§®‡§æ‡§ó‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§¶‡§∞‡•á ‡§§‡§•‡§æ‡•§ ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§û‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ‡§∏‡§§‡§ø ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§Ç ‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§™‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§´‡§≤‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ It should also be noted that the Tamil works call the Pallavas ’tondaiyars’ or ‘kadavars’; they are also described as ‘katu-vettigal (those who cleared the forests for making human settlements). It is surmised that they came to the south from some northern province and began living in the forests. “The Pallavas were almost the first people to acquire dominion in the south. All the tract of country that lay between the Cholas and the Kalingas was formed into a dominion and over this the Pallavas ruled” (K.V. Subrahmanya lyer, Historical Sketches of Ancient Dekhan, Madras, 1917, p.20). There must have been close interaction between the early Pallavas and the early Cholas. Tondaman’s identification as an illegitimate son of a Chola king may not be purely fictitious. He may have been a Pallava, and Pallavas are associated with Tonda-mandalam until the Cholas conquered it in the latter part of the eighth century and renamed Tonda-mandalam as Jayan-gonda-chola mandalam. This may also be the reason why Samavai, who was the daughter of a Pallava chieftain (‘pallavap-perga·∏çe’) and wife of a ‘kadavar’ chief (‘kadavar’ was another name for Pallava), was devoted to the hill-shrine of Tondamandalam. And the Bana kings, who were devoted to this hill-shrine as 24 indicated by their inscriptions, were feudatories of the Pallavas governing their frontier territory (ibid, p.24) According to the information contained in Meckenzie Collection of Manuscripts, Translations and Reports (Vol. XVI, pages 299-314), after Tondaman, seventeen Yadava kings of Narayana-vanam ruled over the region of Torda-mandalam. The Yadavas belonged to the Kurumba tribe, which was patronized and made strong by Tondaman himself. Narayana-vanam was a stronghold of these tribals, and the forest adjoining it was reputed to be frequented by Vishnu. The Kurumbas initially did not have a chieftain of their own, but later selected Komanduru-kurumba-prabhu (probably a Pallava chief) as their lord and king. This king divided the country into twentyfour ‘kottas’ (divisions), and built a fort at Narayana-vanam. A Marathi manuscript (cited by V.N. Srinivas Rao in his Tirupati Sri Venkatesvara-Balaji, Umadevan & Son, Madras 1949, p.87) documents that the rulers of the Yadava dynasty ruled between 809 and 1262 A.D. Among the Yadava kings who ruled over Tonda-mandalam, the names of Sriranga-yadavaraya (about 800 A.D.), Vira-narayana, Vobala, Tiru-vengala, Porumkeyana-Ganda-gopƒÅla (about 900 A.D.), Narasimha, and Viranarasinga (968 - 1000 A.D.) have been preserved. The last named king is said to have built or renovated the hilltemple; he ruled the country from Narayana-vanam and is reputed to The Hill-Shrine have loved to dwell in front of Seshachalam (viz. Vengada hills). The kings had their capitals in Narayana-vanam, Chandragiri and Tirupati, and they were staunch devotees of the god of Kalahasti as well as the Vengadam hill-god. One of the Yadava monarchs, probably Toya-yadava-raya (or Ghatti-deva) is said to have been a contemporary of Ramanuja (1017-1137) and to have been converted by him around 10201029 A.D. He is said to have put the affairs of the hill-shrine in order and arranged for regular worship, with the help of, and under the guidance of Ramanuja, as claimed by Tirumalaiolugu (which is a history of the hill-shrine from Ramanuja’s days till Tiruvenkata-ramanuja-jiyar’s period, 1544, ed. by R Balasundara Nayakar, V.O.I., Tirupati). ‘Chalukya- The later Yadava rulers, NarasingaTirukkalatti-deva (about 1185 A.D.) and Tiruk-kalatti-deva Yadava-raya (1191-1246) described themselves as ‘maharajadhiraja’, narayana’, ‘Godavari-vallabha’ and ‘ventaripalaka’ (‘The protector of the hunter-tribes’). The latter ruler is said to have bestowed upon the hill-shrine the Kudavur village around 1209 A.D. Vira-narasinga Yadava-raya (12091262) was a contemporary of Parantaka I (Sundara-chola), who renovated the sanctum of the hill-shrine. This Yadava king ruled for over fifty years, and his services to the hill-shrine were considerable. Among them was his weighing himself with gold (tularohana) The Hill-Shrine and presenting that gold to gild the vimana. He permitted the pilgrim (desantri), Tiruppullani-dasar, to reconstruct the central vimana of the shrine. His queen-consort (yadava-rayanacchiyar), however, was devoted to Govindaraja-svami of Tirupati, which shrine had been consecrated by Ramanuja in 1130 A.D. A later descendent of his, Hobala-yadava, who was the governor of Ta√±jai (viz., Tanjavur), got a gem-set and gilt copper covering made for the right hand (varada-hasta) of the god of the hills. The hill-temple grew enormously in wealth and celebrity during the reign of the Yadava kings of Tonda-mandalam. The last of the Yadava kings, Ganda-gopala, is said to have ruled between 1250-1285. After the collapse of the Yadava line, the Saluva family of the Vijayanagar empire turned their attention to the hill-shrine. The period between 1359 and 1684 was a golden age for the temple, which became richer than ever, and celebrated in the land. Especially so when Krishnadevaraya was emperor (1509-1530). He visited the shrine seven times and has left 54 inscriptions recording his gifts and the gifts of his two queens. There are altogether 807 inscriptions on the walls of the temple: in Telugu (47), Kannada (24), Tamil (720), Sanskrit (15) and Hindi (1), which record the gifts of land, villages, ornaments, funds for food-offerings and their distribution among pilgrims, rent on land leased out (puliyuttu) and 25 various other sources of income to the temple, made by Pallavas, Cholas, Yadava-rayas, Telugu-Cholas, Telugupallavas and the Vijayanagar kings (cf. published in 6 volumes by T.T.D., between 1931 and 1938). After the collapse of the Vijayanagar empire in 1565 (the battle of Talikota), the affairs of the southern region known as ‘Karnatik’ were in a state of flux and confusion, uncertainty and strife, and several were the contenders who were eager to appropriate the income of the hill-shrine while taking care to preserve the celebrity of the shrine (which had become proverbial goose laying golden eggs). Even during these troubled times, the temple continued to attract large crowds of devotees from all over the country. the Now there are over 30,000 pilgrims on the average that visit the hill-shrine every day of the year. Even in the lean months (usually January and February), there will be an influx of about 20,000 pilgrims daily. During the celebrated Brahmotsavam, there will be more than one lakh each day !In 1961, the pilgrim population was 10.4 lakhs, in 1970 it was 16.4 lakhs, and in 1972 it rose to 18.2 lakhs. In 1981, it became 22-34 lakhs and in 1991 it stood at 29.41 lakhs. The figures provide a fair idea of how increasingly popular the shrine is becoming even in recent years. There was only a small village on top of the hill lying in the hollow surrounded by hills on all sides except east or south-east in close vicinity of the temple as late as 1951; the total population of residents in this village then was only 43. But in 1961, it had already become a town with a population of 5,561. Ten years later, the population rose to 12,879. And in 1981 it was in the neighbourhood of 25,000. The projected population figure for 1991 was 37,3491 (cf. Background paper for Development of Tirumala, Director of Town planning Govt, of A.P., p.10). The area of the township is 1.50 square miles, while the forest surrounding it spreads to 10.33 square miles. Entrance of the pilgrims into the shrine is controlled by a queue complex, which is 9210 feet in length, accommodating about 10,000 pilgrims. Hours of waiting for every one of them has become unavoidable. And one can hardly have a fleeting glance of the deity in the sanctum. Even according to the Workstudy Project Report (Defence Institute of Workstudy, Mussoorie, TTD, Tirupati) of 1976, “The estimated time that a pilgrim gets to stand and pray before the lord is about 4 seconds” (p.21). This was a generous estimate, and only theoretically valid at that. Now after seventeen years, the crowd has enormously increased and the time a pilgrim can get to have a look at the deity has proportionately been reduced, despite the increased hours of queue visits to the shrine (the so called sarva-darsana). 26 The Hill-Shrine (1) CHAPTER TWO HISTORICAL PERSPECTIVE It was mentioned earlier that the hill-god had, since ancient times, the reputation of standing where the hills bent (malai-kuniya-ninran). It is not only that the Vengadam hill is surrounded by higher peaks, but the actual shrine too is located in a depression even on this hill. The level of the area, bounded by the four main streets (called ma·∏ça-streets) around the shrine, is much lower than that of the neighbouring land. The depression is as much as fifteen feet. The shrine faces east; and the street that runs right in front of the temple (called East Mada-Street) is again at a level lower than the street at the back of the temple (West Mada-Street). ground slopes from west to east. The North and South M√£·∏ça-Streets are each of them 800 feet in length, while the West Mada-Street is 900 feet and the East Mada-Street 750. This constitutes what is known as the ‘great circumambulation’ round the shrine (maha-pradakshina), and includes the sacred tank called Svami-pushkarini (or Tiruk-koneri). As one proceeds towards The the shrine from the Sannidhi street (to be described later), there is a flight of steps (now removed) for getting down to reach the East Mada -Street and begin this ‘great circumambulation’. The total area of the shrine as at present is sixteen acres in extent, but the temple itself covers only 2.20 acres out of this large area. The tank (Svami-pushkarini) which is outside the actual temple abutting the North MadaStreet covers 1.50 acres of the area, and another old tank (Pata-pushkarini) which has dried up and is being now used for flower-gardens, cottages, tenements and queue-sheds, also abutting the North Mada Street, covers 2.50 acres. The available area on the hill is in extent 1.2 square miles. Of this area, 0.8% (viz., 6.00 acres) is mountainous, 12.7% (viz., 94.8 acres) is covered by water-stretches, and 44.5% (viz., 345.9 acres) is forest. It is only in the remaining area that we find the temple, the streets around it and the human habitation. The temple is rectangular in shape, and the sides measure 413 feet long and 263 feet wide, It is by no means The Hill Shrine 27 a big temple when compared with the several celebrated temples of South India, especially in Tamil-na·∏çu (like the ones at Chidambaram, Madurai, Tiruvannamalai and Ramesvaram). The streets around the temple are called East Mada-Street (where the main and the only entrance to the temple is situate), South Mada-Street, west side prakaram lane behind the temple, and very narrow) and the Tirtha-katta street (on the northern side, adjoining the sacred tank (tirtha-katte) known as Svami-pushkari≈Üi. It is a time-honoured custom for the pilgrim (now, alas, observed by very few indeed) to enter the shrine only after walking along the four Mada streets, always keeping the shrine to his right. This is, as mentioned earlier, known as ‘maha-pradakshina (the great circumambulation), and includes, besides the shrine, the sacred tank (Svami-pushkari≈Üi), Varaha-svami temple and the original village of Tirumala. The temple as it stands now, and curiously enough as described in the Varaha-purƒÅ·πáa (7,55-57) as built by Tondaman, has One pradakshina marga and two enclosing walls (prakaras) and two entrances surmounted by towers (gopuras), and a sanctum (garbha-griha). ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§∏‡§Ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ó‡•ã‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§¢‡§Ø‡§∏‡§Ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§ó‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§∞‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç‡§Æ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã‡§¶‡•ç‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ Bhavishyottara-purana (13, 31f) descri bes the temple that Tondaman built more elaborately, and probably the description is based on the structures that the author actually saw (in the fourteenth or fifteenth century). All the apartments that the purana mentions can really be found within the precincts of the temple (except perhaps the ‘seven doors’). ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§µ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡•á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§ö‡•à‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•Å‡§≠‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§ó‡•ã‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§∏‡§Ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•• ‡§∏‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§µ‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•á‡§§‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•á‡§π‡§≤‡•Ä‡§§‡•ã‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç|| ‡§Ü‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§™‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡•Å‡§§‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó‡§Æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§™‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§ó‡•ã‡§∂‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§§‡§§‡•ã ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ‡§ï‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ ‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ó‡•á‡§π‡§Ç ‡§§‡•à‡§®‡§∂‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ò‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ï‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§≠‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∂‡§æ‡§≤‡•§ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ ‡§ö ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§∑‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡§Ç ‡§§‡§•‡§æ‡•§‡•§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡§ó‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ú‡§æ‡§≤‡§§‡•à‡§≤‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ || ‡§ï‡§≤‡•ç‡§™‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∂‡•Å ‡§ö ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡•á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§ó‡§ú‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ï‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ The Puranic account, which puts this description in the mouth of the god himself (instruction to king Tondaman) is obviously misleading, for these apartments were actually built at different periods of time. They might have been there when the author of the ‘Venkatachala-mahatmya’ section of the purana composed the verses; but such was certainly not the temple that Tondaman built. That the temple complex which we find find now (and in essence as the purana describes) has evolved during several centuries is known by textual and inscriptional references. 28 The Hill Shrine While it is not difficult to reconstruct the stages in the shrine’s development or to ascertain their periods, it is not easy to determine the social and religious forces at work during the long history of the temple. There are several aspects of the temple’s architecture which are unusual and enigmatic, as for instance, the closure of the first circumambutatory path (mukkoti-pradakshina). We are often unsure if the inscriptional references to some of the additional structures in the temple pertain to altogether new structures or to renovations of old structures. We hear of major and extensive renovations in the thirteenth century, but how the temple looked before this period can hardly be ascertained. The central shrine (consisting of the sanctum, the antarala, and the mukha-mandapa) must have been the nucleus of the temple in strict conformity with the agama conception of temples. The Mukha-Mantapa (known now as Mahamani-mantapa) constructed in 1417 A.D. by Madhavadasar of Chandragiri must have made provision for a circumambutatory path (now called Mukkoti-pradakshina or Vaikuntha-pradakshina), which has now been closed The rest of the temple evolved in course of time as necessitated by needs..,it different periods of time; the structures that were added after 1417 AD were not directly related to the central shrine, nor were they interconnected organically. The Hill Shrine (2) It is impossible for a pilgrim at present to obtain an idea of the temple as a whole; he is confined to the queue lines, and has little opportunity to observe either the art or architectural peculiarities of this temple. The administration too has altered many of the structures to suit contemporary needs and ideas. Therefore, an account of the ‘denominations, measurements and utility of the various apartments and structures” in the temple officially prepared in 1818 (for the East India Company which took possession of this temple) would be the best guide to get a detailed picture of the temple. This is known as the Paimayishi Account and was prepared by the Karnams (administrative officers on the spot) or the temple. The account is here reproduced from Sadhu Subrahmanya Sastry’s Tirupati Sri Venkatesvara (T.T.D., Tirupati, 1981, pp. 324-326). “In Venkatesvara’s temple in Tirumala, it (Paimayishi Account) notices the garbha-grham (sactum sactorum) with the Vimanam (dome) over it, the Antaralam (walls or roof) and the Mukha-mantapam (front portico) as item No.1, then the mancapu-sevagrham (the apartment in which the cot is suspended from the ceiling for the bed of the Deity), next the Sri Ramacandrasvami-sannidhi (the high pial where Sri Rama was stationed). adjoining it on the east Mukha-an- 29 or tarala-mantapam (front terraced walled portico) and lastly the Rangamantapam (a pavilion for public show of functions and services to the Deity, the unwalled Mahamani-mantapam) with an inner circuit (to pradaksinam) commencing at south-east and passing around the sanctum from the dolumantapam (for the play of the drummer and pipers) and ending on the north-side with the shrine of SenaMudaliyar and the adjoining exit mantapam. All these together form the central shrine. To the south east of this group, stands the Svayam-pakasala or Melpotu (kitchen); from this westwards upto the Kalyana-mantapam (pavilion for the performance of the marriage of the Deity) is a passage with a line of 10 stone pillars in front and three rooms behind, one for the lamplighter, next the Yagasala (Sacrificial room for oblations to the Fire-God); and the third in charge of Mehtaji Sahukar. The Kalyana-mantapam is said to comprise a thin sculptured double-pillared Simhasana (seating)mantapam, a small shrine for lodging Malayappasvami (Processional Image) during the Brahmotsavam, and a small Garbha-grham behind it. All these stand on a high platform to the south of the Vimana-Pradaksinam (corridor around the central shrine with the Vimana (dome) over the sanctum). The platform on the western side of this corridor is said to contain rooms (1) for Raghoji Bhonsle’s pallaki (palan- 30 quin) and Vahanalu (vehicles), (2) for the vahanalu of the Nadigaddam family men, (3) Surapuram state’s Vahanams and other things, (4) Venkatagiri Raja’s prasadam distribution, (5) in Padagala family’s charge, and (6) room for extraction of civet oil. On the platform to the north of the Vimana-Pradaksinam were rooms occupied serially by the cooks, the Mysore state for provisions, the Matla chief’s people, the Kaikala servant, Vagapadi (offered panyarams), Tallapakam musicians inscribed copperplates, Sabha-ara for keeping the Deities’ cloths’, Bhasyakara’s shrine, mancapu-ara (room for keeping the Deity’s cot), the Kanuka-ara (room for keeping donations from the pilgrims), and the Bokkasam people’s panyaram the sandal-grinding room, temple-prasadam-keeping room, and to its south at the east end the shrine of Yoga-Narasimhasvami. room, At the south-east corner between the inner door-way under the samller tower and the kitchen stands Varadarajasvami’s shrine. The darvaja (central gate) is noted to be 33 feet from east to west, 21 feet from north to south and 15 feet high, with the gopuram of three tiers of plaster surmounted by Kalasams, (small ornamental vases) of 33 feet in length from east to west, 21 feet in breadth from north to south and 51 feet in height.   34. Garu·∏çalvar (in Tirumamani-man- tapa) 32 The Hill Shrine Dvara-palakas (in Tirumamani-mantapa) The spots within the main shrine: 40. Bangaru-vakili (entrance door for the shrine)

  36. Kulasekharap-padi (door-step of the sanctum) (3) are of The temple owns numerous flowergardens (nanda-vanams) which which spread around the township Tirumalai. From very ancient times, decorating the deity of the hill-shrine with flowers and sandal paste has been a well-known feature. We read about it in ancient Tamil calssics and in the songs of the Alvars. In fact, tradition ascribes the first flower-garden as an accessory to the temple to the time when the deity was discovered and consecrated. The puranas tell us that Ranga-dasa in the distant past took upon himself the task of supplying everyday flower-garlands to the god of the hills, which was being worshipped by the Vaikhanasa priest Gopinatha. Ranga-dasa and Gopinatha were the builders of the first shrine, which must have been a simple one; the flower garden was close by. Ranga-dasa was reborn as the prince Tondaman, who was, according to the Puranas, responsible for a magnificent temple, and also for numerous flower-gardens. At present there are about three hundred temple-gardens from which flowers are supplied. About fifty-three gardens have inscriptional references. The earliest was Arisinalaiyan tirunanda-vanam started by Pillai Tiru venkatach-chirukkar in 1390 A.D., while the last inscriptional reference to a garden was in 1606 A.D. However, a garden mentioned in an inscription of 1250 A.D. (Moita-s≈çlai tiru-nanda vanam, set up by Mullai Tiru-venkatajiyar) was in Tirupati. In 1848, there thirty-seven nandavanams tached to the temple, but by 1905 many of them had disappeared into the adjoining jungles. A note dated 1906 (D. Dis. No. 170 dated 3-111906) granted the area of 10 1/3 square miles to the temple, conferring the right to use the flowers and fuel within this area. were at- It is likely that Samavai (Kadavanperundevi) who got made the silver replica of the hill-god and instituted some festivals in 614 A.D. also arranged for a flower-garden on the hill. There was a ‘perundevi-totam’ on the northern side of Varahasvami temple until recently. However, the first garden attached to the hill-shrine in the recognizable past was set up by the first citizen of the village on top of the hill, Tirumala-nambi (about 10001074). Also known as Sri-saila-pur·πáa, he was the grandson of the celebrated Sri-vaishnava teacher Yamuna-muni The Hill Shrine 33 (Alavandar) and the maternal uncle of the great Ramanuja. He heard one day his grandfather explain the eulogy of Nammalvar the hill-god by (Tiruvaymoli, ten verses of the third ten of the third centum); he was just twenty years of age then, but decided immediately that he should visit Vƒìngadam. When he did visit, he resolved to settle down permanently on the hill and grow a flower-garden as service for the deity. He lived there alone for a long time. He is supposed to have taught the hidden meaning of Ramayana to Ramanuja for a year. He would everyday come down to the foot of the hills for this purpose, as Ramanuja was living in Kottur (which was later absorbed into Tirupati). The spot where he taught was under a tamarind tree on the foot-path to the hill, and a shrine to Ramanuja was subsequently built there. Anandalvar (also called. Anantalvan, born in 1046 A.D.), who was a disciple of Ramanuja, joined Tirumala-nambi and took up the same service (nandavana-kainkarya). He dug up a new tank as watersource for the flower garden. This fresh-water tank is now known as Alvar-cheruvu, not far from the temple, to the north of the road which leads to Chandra-giri. The garden that Anandalvar set up was known as Purusha-vari-t≈çtam. His son and grandson continued this service to the god; there are several inscriptions (No. 171 to 175 in vol I) to this effect by his grandson Venkatat-turaivar. Anandalvar’s descendents are to this dayassociated with the hill-shrine ‘acharya-purushas’. as The Kararu-nama executed on the 21st of April 1843 by the then administrator of the hill-shrine (mahant), Seva-dasa of Hathiramjimatha, mentions several several ‘pagodas’ (viz.shrines) of minor importance (on the hill) which he took possession of, along with ornaments, clothes, vehicles, idols and all other property belonging to each of them.

This is an interesting document, for it provides an insight into the landmarks of the then village of Tirumala, besides the main shrine. During kartika-dipotsava, lamps are lit at forty-two spots in the hill-shrine, as already mentioned, and of them thirty-six are within the temple precincts; the remaining six are lit outside the temple and at the spots identified as Dova-bhashyakara, Gogarbha-kshetrapalaka, Bedi-Anja neya, Pushkarini - Anjaneya, Varahasvami and Svami-pushkarini. These are the very ‘pagodas’ mentioned in the Kararu-nƒÅmƒÅ executed by Mahant Seva-dasa in 1843 (excepting Svamipushkarini, which is substituted by the Ramanuja shrine in the VimanaPradakshina). The customary kartikadipa in a temple suggests the limits of the town or village. The six spots for dipa prescribed in the Tirumala Devasthanam’s Nitya-dittam (T.T.D., reprinted 1963, pp. 33-35) indicates of the the customary landmarks Tirumala village. The Kshetra-pala is actually a huge stone believed to be the guardan-spirit of the place, which was formerly kept near the present Dhvaja-stambha-mantapa, outside the inner prakara. It was Kshetra-Pala-Gundu in Gogarbha-Tirtha THO 35 believed that, as the shrine was in the midst of a wild forest and as there was no human habitation near the shrine, the Kshetra-pala-gundu would roam about during daytime and come back at night. The priest, who visited the shrine everyday, would lock the doors of the shrine in the evening, and place the keys on the Kshetrapala-gundu before he got down the hill. He would find them safe there, when he arrived at the shrine the next morning. It became the custom to worship this stone periodically during nights, when the village of Tirumala took shape. The custom was observed all along the history of the temple, until recently, when the stone was shifted to a spot near the Gogarbhatirtha, about a mile to the north-east of the temple. The reason for this shifting has not been made explicit. But the temple administration still arranges for worship of this stone in Gogarbha-tirtha on Sivaratri night every year.

However, for an official document to mention this stone as a ‘pagoda’ and for the Mahant to be interested in taking possession of it, it must have been of considerable importance in the village. It is usual for Kshetrapala shrines to be located on the outskirts of a settlement; and being a guardianspirit it is usually fierce in nature, demanding animal sacrifices. In the case of Tirumala, the village first grew at the back of the temple and towards the northern side, as evidenced by the location of the West Ma·∏ça-street and North Mada-street, as well as by the location of the Alvar-cheruvu (the water-source for the village). The two streets mentioned above enclosed the original village. The Kshetrapala stone, situated outside the enclosure known Sampangi-pradakshina probably denoted the limits of the village on the eastern side (including the shrine within the limits of the village). as A medieval work, Sri-Venkatesaseva-krama, by Gargya-Sathari (which will be discussed later) was composed when the temple had only the enclosure known now as Vimanapradakshina. Outside the stone walls of this enclosure was an uncovered and unenclosed circumambulatory path called Champaka-vithika (pathway named Champaka, or Sampangipradakshina), because of the Champaka-tree which stood there (verse 64). ‡§Ü‡§ó‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ö‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§ï‡§§‡§∞‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§ø‡§§‡§µ‡•Ä‡§•‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡•§ The pilgrim who went round the shrine along this open pathway would see the sacred tank Svami-pushkari≈Üi to his left (verse 66) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ö‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§§‡§É ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ before he again reached the bali-pitha in front of what now is the inner dvara-gopura and outside the inner prakara walls (verse 68): 36 The Hill Shrine ‡§¨‡§≤‡§ø‡§™‡•Ä‡§†‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ó‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§ö‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§ï‡§Ç ‡•§ His circumambulation which had started from this bali-pitha (which is even now in the same spot) ends there, before he enters into the Vimana-pradakshina. It is clear from the account that what now is the inner gopura was, when the work was composed, the main entrance, (mahadvara). The precincts of the temple were bounded by the outer prakara walls of the Vimana-pradashi≈Üa. outer Thus the Kshetra-pala stone was originally outside the temple. When, however, the the Sampangi-pradakshina too was enclosed, and an was constructed (probably gateway around 1217 A.D., for an inscription of the Yadava-prince Vira-narasingadevar is found on this gopuram), its location was included in the shrine area, and it was no longer on the outskirts of the village. And its independent identity was also lost: it could no longer be propitiated with the customary food -offerings by the village folk. It was in this context that it was probably shifted near Gogarbha-tirtham, to accommodate the possible expansion of the village on this side of the temple. Of the other ‘minor pagodas’, Varaha-svami-shrine and one of the two Anjaneya-shrines are associated with the sacred tank, Svami-pushkari≈Üi. The sacred tank itself is outside the precincts of the temple, and its water The Hill Shrine is not used in the temple for any purpose. It is a long-standing custom that water from Akasa-ganga, about a mile away, is brought for the Friday abhishekam for the hill-god; and water from the well inside the temple, known as Bangaru-bavi, was used until recently for other requirements in the temple. It is also to be noted that none of the Alvars who visited the shrine has mentioned this sacred tank, or the Varaha-svami shrine on its bank; nor does any early inscription refer to either of them. The tank is of course eulogized in the Puranas. Among the seven most important water-sources on the hill, Brahma-purana(4,7) counts this tank as the first. Skanda-purana (3,49-50) says: ‡§∏‡•Å‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§∞‡•Ä‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•á ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡•á ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ ‡§™‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•á ‡•§ ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§π‡§∞‡•á ‡§™‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§ß‡§æ‡§§‡•Å‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•á ‡•§‡•§ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä ‡§ö‡•á‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§™‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§π‡§ø ‡§Æ‡§Ç‡§ó‡§≤‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§Æ‡§Ç‡§ó‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡§ø‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§™‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§∂‡•ã‡§ß‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§‡•§ According to the puranic accounts, the sacred tank was there much earlier than Varaha-svami’s shrine was built on its banks; and Venkatesa appeared on the scene much later. This latter tank has really been of great use in Tirumala for over eighthundred years now. Among the works undertaken by the first Mahant was to fill Svami-pushkari≈Üi, which had become almost dry, with water from Pata-pushkari≈Üi (also called Achyutaraya-k≈çnƒìru) on the west through underground channels. It is probable that a small shrine was built for this deity around that time; but there is little to show that it was of any importance on the hill in subsequent years. But the attention devoted by Sri-vaishnava enthusiasts on Varaha was not entirely absent, for The Hill Shrine Varaha-SvƒÅmi Shrine on the banks of SvƒÅmi-Pushkari≈Üi Varaha is one of the three principal manifestations of Godhead (mukhyavrtti, the other two being Nrsimha and Hayagriva) in the Srivaishnava theology, and was the foundation for the Vijnana-maya-kosha of human constitution (and hence called Jnanappiran). An inscription of 1476 elevates this Varaha-nayanar to the status of Jnanap-piran (’tiruk-k≈çnƒìri karayil jnanappiran sannidiyil iramanujayyan tirup-pu-mandapattil’). Very soon, the deity was addressed as Adi-varahaperumal (in an inscription of 1481). But in 1801 the East India Company did not consider it worthwhile to take over the administration of this temple, for its income was less than Rs. 250 in a year! 40 Varaha-Svami The Hill Shrine The Hill Shrine Varaha-Svami in the sanctum of the shrine 41When in 1900, Mahant Prayagadasa became the administrator of the Tirumala temples he found the Varahasvami temple in a terrible state of dilapidation and neglect. For over ten years, the Varaha-svami idol had to be protected in the Aina-mahal (the enclosed mantapam to the right of the main gateway as one enters) of the hill-shrine. It was this Mahant that took pity on this Adi-varahap-perumal and got his shrine repaired and renovated; the image was shifted back to its original sanctum! The sacred tank was not in a happier position either; never of much use in the village, it was also in a state of utter neglect. This Mahant turned his attention to this tank, and got a Nirali-mandapam (also called Vasanta-mandapam) constructed within the tank, for occasional spectacular festivals like the tirthavari (ablutions) on the ninth day of Brahmotsava (tirokkodi-tirunal) and Margali-nirattam which had become popular in the fifteenth and sixteenth centuries. The Mahant was obviously thinking of tourist attractions and revenue. a While the Varaha-svami shrine had a background and did become landmark in Tirumala, the Anjaneya shrine in front of it, which was sought to be projected by the Mahant as another landmark failed to attract any attention, despite the claim that Vyasaraya-svami (born 1446 A.D.) worshipped it. It is likely that the shrine 42 to Anjaneya was built by Vyasa-rayasvami on the plot of land donated to him by the Vijayanagar monarch Krishnadeva-raya close to the sacred tank (’tirumalai-melil-tiruk-k√∂n√™rikku’) January 12, 1524. on The other Anjaneya shrine mentioned in the Mahant’s deed of execution of 1843 fared better. This was the one that marked the beginning of the Sannidhi Street, the only link-road for the pilgrims who ascended the hill from the Tirupati side and the one that led the pilgrim to the main entrance of the hill-shrine, and came therefore easily to the attention of the Anjaneya in front of Varahasvami shrine on the banks of Svami-pushkari≈Üi The Hill Shrine pilgrim. Known as Be·∏çi-Hanuman (viz. Anjaneya in chains’), called so for an unknown reason (although there is a fanciful story of how the hill-god chained the monkey-god’s feet and prevented his being constantly on the move), he stands directly facing the hill-god. This shrine was formerly contiguous with other structures adjoining the thousand-pillared-hall’ (sahasrastambha-mantapa), which is on the left side of the Sannidhi-street. There was a passage through which the pilgrims could enter and this was by the side of this shrine. Thus, the pilgrim would worship at this shrine before he walked along the Sannidhi street. When the street was widened in 1970 by the TTD, the Bedi-Hanuman shrine was isolated, and this is almost a modern structure now. In any case, this was one of the landmarks in the old temple area which began with the Sannidhistreet, and included the thousand-pillared mantapam. Then the document mentions two shrines for Ramanuja (Bhashyakara) as ‘minor pagodas’ one within the temple complex and the other, known as Dova-Bhashyakara-shrine, on the summit of the stiff flight of steps (called ‘knee-breaker’, ‘mo·πáa-kal-murippu’). Ramanuja (1017-1137 A.D.), the great acharya and leader of the Sri-vaishnavacommunity had much to do with the regularization of worship in the hill-shrine, and has left a deep imprint on the village on the plains, Kottur, which he renamed Tirupati. He is said The Hill Shrine Bƒì·∏çi-Anjaneya to have initiated many trends in the development of the village on the hill-top, although there is not a single inscriptional reference to the work he did in the hill-shrine or in the village atop the hill. We have to rely entirely on sectarian works like Tirumalai-olugu, Guru-paramparƒÉ, Koil-olugu and 43 which do not always distinguish fact from fiction. to In any case, the Sri-vaishnava community of Tirupati was eager to set claims on the hill-shrine, which was a stronghold of the Vaikhanasa priests, and set up these two shrines to the Ramanuja. About shrine Ramanuja within the hill-shrine (in the Vimana-pradakshina), we do not hear of it until about 1476 A.D., although there was a shrine to Ramanuja in the village below the hill (Tirupati) as early as 1220 A.D. And this shrine on the hill has never been treated as an Anjaneya (Bedi-Hanuman) Installed by Saluva-Narasimha in the Sannidhl street, facing the temple of the hill-god independent one, and did not at any time mean the ascendance of the usual Pancharatragama in the hill-shrine. It cannot be ascertained how or why the Mahant described it as a minor pagoda, which he was anxious to take control of. Being a part of the temple-complex on the hill, it would naturally be under his administrative control. The possible reason may be that this shrine was being managed by the Sri-vaishnava community from Tirupati, the Vaikhanasa priests having no interest in it whatsoever. Although within the temple-complex, it may have been treated as a separate entity during the days of the East India Company. The other shrine to Ramanuja (the Dova-Bhashyakara-sannidhi which was erected in 1485 A.D.) now served as a landmark in the outer periphery of the Tirumala village, signifying the pilgrim’s entry into the sacred circuit on top of the hill. It should also be remembered that the Mahant had about this time represented before the Board of Revenue that he be given “a circular tract of two square miles in extent having the temple in the centre, and a radius of 1404 yards, and also as regards the roads leading to lower Tirupati, Chandragiri, Balepalli and the water-falls” (which came up for hearing only during 1876 before D.F. Carmichael, Secretary to Government). Inclusion of Dova-Bhashyakarasannidhi among the minor pagodas was 44 The Hill Shrine –°–ª 5 probably meant to strengthen the Mahant’s claim. (4) Despite the availability of a large number of inscriptions recording numerous royal benefactions and providing information about a variety of festivals newly instituted and a richer variety of food offerings, arranged for different occasions, it is hard to reconstruct the religious history of the hill-shrine. The elaborate puranic accounts no doubt offer some clues, but they are not sufficient. Books like K≈çil-olugu, Tirumalai-olugu, and Venkatachala-mƒÅhƒÅthmyam are sec- tarian in orientation and give details only suited to their religious inclinations. The documents got prepared by the East India Company when they took over the administration in 1748 A.D. (the well-known Meckenzie manuscripts, the Dittams, the Kainkaryapattis, Bruce’s Code, the Sawal-Jawab Patti, the Paimayishi account etc.) throw light on the conditions (more administrative and economic than religious) prevailing then, and have limited interest in the religious character of the temple. Two details, however, stand out as almost undisputed. One is the influence exerted by the great Srivaishnava teacher Ramanuja (10171137 A.D.), who visited the shrine two or three times during his long life; and the other is the persistence of the The Hill Shrine Vaikhanasa mode of worship in the temple, from very ancient times till today, in spite of the impact of Ramanuja (who was an enthusiastic supporter of the Pancharatra system of worship) and his followers (not only the temple functionaries like the ekangi, jiyar, acharya-purusha, dasanambi, koil-kelvi and kaikkolar but royal patrons like the Yadava-rayas and the Vijayanagar monarchs). The Vaikhanasas are zealous worshippers of Vishnu but do not follow Ramanuja; they do not recognize Pancharatra as a valid method of worhsip. And they have never been other than templepriests, and do not form a powerful or influential community. The hill-shrine was dominated for long years by members of the Sri-vaishnava community from Tirupati, Sri-rangam and Kanchipuram after the thirteenth century. Unlike the Vaikhanasa priests, the Sri-vaishnava managers and supervisors of the hill-shrine had political support. It is remarkable in the circumstances that the Vaikhanasa hold over the worship details never weakened, and that there was very little confrontation between the two groups in the history of the hill-shrine. Rarnanuja’s association with the hill-shrine began with his visit to the hill as a young man to learn the hidden meanings of Ramayana from his maternal uncle, (brother of his mother Bhu-piratti), Tirumala-nambi, who had taken up residence on the 45 hill to raise a flower-garden for the service of the hill-god. The traditional accounts insist that Ramanuja did not go up the hill for this purpose, but stayed on the plains; Tirumala-nambi used to descend the hills to reach Ramanuja. The Ramayana sessions took place under a tamarind tree, and were carried on for a year. Although Tirumala-nambi was the grandson of the illustrious predecessor of Ramanuja, Yamuna-muni (or Alavandar A.D., 918-1038 A.D.) and was conversant with the temple-rituals and temple -administraton in Sri-rangam and Kanchipuram, he never interfered with either the customary worship that was carried on in the temple or with the administration of the temple. He confined his attention to the duty that he had himself chosen to provide flowers and water for the daily_worship of the gud, whom all the Alvars had glorified. The second time that Ramanuja visited the hill-shrine was after he had become the Acharya or leader of the Sri-vaishnava community in Sri-rangam (hence called Emperumanar or U·∏çaiyavar); it is said that Tirumalanambi was still living at that time. The books mentioned above say that Ramanuja visited the hill-shrine for the third time in his extreme old age, a year or two before he passed away, and that the Yadava-king Ghattiga (or Ghatti-deva) who ruled over the region, patronized him and carried out the changes in the temple according 46 to his directions. It is said that this king ruled from Narayana-vanam as a feudatory of the Chola monarch Kulottunga-chola II (1123-1146). And the books, sectarian as they are, especially the Tirumalai-olugu), narrate in great detail how Ramanuja converted the hill-shrine into an unmistakably vaishnavite one. Among the changes instituted by Ramanuja, the following are noted in them: 1. 2. 3. 4. 5. 6. 7. he identified the deity as Vish≈Üu; he provided the deity with the usual emblems in the two upper hands, samkha and chakra, which were then absent; he bestowed a golden pendant with the image of Tirumal-nacchiyar (viz. Lakshmi) to be hung round the deity’s neck; he arranged for the Friday bath for the deity (tiru-ma√±janam); he prescribed the characteristic Vaishnava mark on the forehead (urdhva-pun·∏çra); he got the vimana over the sanctum (Ananda-nilaya) repaired and also got the forms of Vishnu, Varaha, Narasimha, Vaikunthanatha and Venkatesa installed on its four sides, as also the figures of Vishnu’s attendants, Ananta, Garuda, Vishvaksena and dvara-palakas; the he organized the flower-gardens on the hill (nanda-vana); the garden tended by Tirumala-nambi, his uncle, had already gone into disuse: he therefore asked Tirumala- The Hill Shrine nambi’s grandson Tolappan tatacharya to look after this garden; 8. he installed the processional deity, Malayappan (which is now being used), in place of Ugra-srinivasa (about which there will be an occasion to deal with later); 9. he appointed a mendicant-disciple of his, Satha-k≈çpa-yati, to supervise the worship in the temple, and an ekangi was named as a helper. The account giving the above details is certainly not a historical one, and the details themselves are open to serious misgivings. The sectarian prejudices have freely played their role; and there are more conjectures and fictions than a faithful narration of actual events. For instance, the story about the identification of the hill-god as Vishnu presupposes a tussle between the Saivas and the Vaishnavas. There is no evidence that the character of the hill-god was ever disputed; nor is there evidence for the occurrence of an encounter between the Saivite and Vaishnavite factions on the hill at that time, or at anytime for that matter. Such encounters became prevalent only in the fourteenth and fifteenth centuries in the Tamil country; and then they raised an unwholesome dust of discontent, animosity and suspicion. It was probably under this influence that such stories were invented and projected back to Ramanuja’s days. There was no king Ghattiga by name in the Yadava line of kings who ruled over the northern and eastern divisions of Tonda-mandalam; the lists documented in the Meckenzie Manuscripts (vol XVI, pp. 390-414) mention Toya-yadava (1008-1020), Talalu-gottina-raya (1020-1029) and Vasudeva-raya (1029-1041) as contemporaneous with Ramanuja. There was a Ghatti-deva among the Yadava-kings, (the father of Raja-malla). He ascended the throne only in 1184 A.D., long after the passing away of Ramanuja. But these are mere names, and we know little about what the Yadavakings did until we come to Tirukkalatti-deva Yadava-raya (1191-1246 A.D.) who ruled the region of Tonda-mandalam with Venkata-giri as capital, and who bestowed the Ku·∏çavur village to the hill-shrine as a source of revenue in 1209 A.D., long after Ramanuja passed away. His successor Viranarasinga Yadava-raya (1209 probable date of his birth-1262 A.D.) was a contemporary of the famous Chola monarch Parantaka II (Sundara-chola), who renovated the vimana of the hill-shrine. Vira-narasinga is said to have weighed himself against gold (tularohana) and to have presented this gold for gilt-plating of the Vimana. His wife, Yadava-raya-nacchiyar, was a devotee of the GovindarajƒÖ shrine in Tirupati, which had been established by Ramanuja in 1130 A.D. (24th February). It was probably after the days of Vira-narasinga that the in- The Hill Shrine 47 fluence of the Sri-vaishnava community began to be felt on the hill. The ‘reforms’ in the hill-shrine that are ascribed to Ramanuja may actually have been the doings of the powerful Sri-vaishnava lobby during the later Yadava-rayas, especially during the reigns of Vira-narasinga-deva, GandaGopala (1250-1285) and Sri-ranganatha Yadava-raya (1336-1367). What Ramanuja did was probably only to organize the flower-gardens (nandavanams) on the hill and associate his disciples with them. The expression ‘matham’ being usually linked in the inscriptions (e.g. TT 354) with the nanda-vanams is very suggestive. The owners of the gardens were also heads of ‘mathas’ as, for instance, Emperumanar-Jiyar (who was also a K≈çil-kelvi, a temple functionary in the hill-shrine) who is mentioned as such in an inscription of 1445 A.D. In fact, it was the manager of Arisinalayamnandavanam (made over by the original owner Singanna Dannayaka, commander of the army of Hoysala Vira-Ballala), who was also the head of a matha, Mullai Tiru-venkata-jiyar (1375-1445), who established the Periya-jiyar - matham, which is very much involved in the worship-rituals in the hill-shrine. His successor, Emperumanar-jiyar, (1445-1493) who looked after Pa≈àkayachchelli-nandavanam, has been called in an inscription (TT, 106, 48, 15) ‘k≈çyilkelvi’ (temple-functionary). After his 48 as death, Ramanuja-jiyar was the manager of the same flower-garden as well as a temple functionary. And the jiyar that came after him, Anusandhanam Tiruvenkata-jiyar (1504-1520) managed a flowergarden after his own name, Tiru-venkata-nathan-nandavanam, well as another, Per-arulalan-nandavanam. It was during his time that a junior Jiyar (another k≈çil-kelvi) was also there to assist him in the hillshrine; and so he came to be known as Periya-Jiyar. It is thus that the Jiyar-matham came to be associated with the hill-shrine in an important manner. The image of Ramanuja is claimed to have been installed in the hillshrine around 1248-49 A.D., during the days of Vijaya-ganda-gopala (D.No. 79), over a hundred years after the passing away of the great teacher. The claim that Ramanuja himself established the Jiyar-matham in 1047 A.D. to supervise and guide the worship rituals in the hill-shrine can hardly be sustained, as we have shown above. But it is a fact that there was an increasing influence of the Sri-vaishnava ideology in the hill-shrine. The residence of the processional image of Ranganatha of Sri-rangam (along with the leaders of the Sri-vaishnava community) within the temple-complex on this hill (in the present Ranga-nayakulamandapam) for several years during its travel from 1368 (when it was taken out of Srirangam to protect it from the imminent invasion by the Muslim army The Hill Shrine of Malik-kafur) to 1371 (when it was brought back to Sri-rangam when the place was safe) was was another circumstance that made the Sri-vaishnava ideas and practices familiar to the Vaikhanasa priests. Likewise, the processional images of Varada-raja of Kanchipuram and Narasimha of Sholinghur are said to have found refuge in this hill-shrine at different periods, although evidence for such residence is not there. Even before this date, the Vaikhanasa priests had agreed to accommodate some of the Pancharatra practices (of the Sri-vaishnava brand in the hill-shrine. The images of Vishnu with two) consorts (ubhayanachchiyar, ≈öri-devi and Bhu-devi) appeared in the hill-shrine for the first time in 1339, during the third regnal year of Sri-ranga-natha Yadava-raya (inscriptions, I, 104). Considering that this was an ’eka-bera’ temple (the sanctum housing the lone image of Vishnu), and considering that even the silver iconic replica of the hill-god which was got made by Samavai in 614 A.D. and installed in the ’tiruvilankoil ‘of the hill-shrine was a lone figure (without consorts), this was a big concession indeed. And permission to sing the Tamil verses of Nammalvar’s Tiruvaymoli before the hill-god in 1360 (inscriptions, 1, 107) was another concession given to the Pancharatra enthusiasts. As mentioned earlier, a shrine to Ramanuja was already there within the temple-precincts, although no inde- The Hill Shrine pendent worship was allowed there; but shrines to the Alvars did not come up in the hill-shrine or in its vicinity. During the reign of Vira-narasingadeva Yadava-raya (1209-1262) .there was an extensive renovation of the hill-shrine, as indicated by an inscription (No. 17 TT) on the north wall of the first enclosure of the hill-shrine. This was at the instance of a Srivaishnava pilgrim from outside the region (desantri), Tiruppullani-dasar of the Tamil country (Seravan-mahadevichaturvedi-mangalam, the present day Shermadevi). The renovation work is said to have begun around 1245 A.D. But what changes were actually carried out at that time cannot be known. But there is a reference in the inscription to some work done with regard to the sanctum. The words of the inscription are: k≈çvil-alvarukku k≈çvilukku k≈çvil seyya’. There is a play on the word “K√µvil’ (k≈ç-il,“great house”), which generally means shrine or temple. The ‘alvar’ of the ‘K≈çvil’ is of course the god housed in the shrine-sanctum. The expression speaks of a shrine being constructed for the god of the shrine (‘kovil-alvarukku’) as also for the shrine itself (‘kovilukku’). It may refer to the erection of a vimanam over the sanctum, for a vimanam is architecturally indicative of the location of the sanctum and the crowning glory of the temple-complex. The expression ‘seyya’ in the inscription literally means ‘doing’, 49 ‘constructing’. It suggests that there was no vimanam for the hill-shrine till that date. If the Tamil expression is used figuratively it may mean ‘renovation’, ‘reconstruction’, in which case the vimanam that was already there needed repair and renovation. It is not improbable that the sanctum had some sort of a superstructure before the date of this inscription, but we do not hear of a regular vimana being there earlier. To bear the burden of a new and grand vimana, the walls of the sanctum had also to be reinforced or built anew. The renovation mentioned in the inscription may refer to the reconstruction of the sanctum as well as erecting a vimana over it. We find that not long after this event the vimana was covered by copper plates and gilt. Vira-narasingadeva got himself weighed against gold (tularohana) and presented this gold for purposes of gilding the vimana (TT 37, No. 19). He was a feudatory of Jata-varma Sundara-pandya I, who ascended the throne in 1251 A D and ruled over the entire peninsula till the river Godavari. Defeating the Cholas, Pallavas, Kakatiyas and the Andhra kings, this Pandyan monarch anointed himself cremoniously (virabhisheka) at Kanchipuram. To mark his victory, he also got a golden vase or kalasam fixed on the gold-plated vimanam of the hill-shrine, which Viranarasinga-deva Yadava raya, his vassal, had just then completed (TT No. 172). Both these events (gold-plating and fixing the Kalasam) took place between the years 1260 and 1262. It was during the days of Viranarasinga-deva Yadava-raya, therefore, that the hill-shrine became glorious like the mount Sumeru. The Yadava rulers were devotees of this hill-shrine and also the shrine at Kalahasti, but the inscriptions during the later Yadava rulers frequently seek “the protection of Sri-vaishnavas” and mention “permission of Sri-vaishnavas” and “feeding the ≈öri-vaishnavas”, and Tirupati becomes increasingly important. There came about also an administrative council (sthanattar) to manage the financial and other affairs of the hill-shrine. Of the ten or twelve members of this council, around A.D., the majority were Sri-vaishnavas of Tirupati and Tiruchanur, (including K≈çil-kelku Jiyars and Koil-kanakku tiruninrai-u·∏çaiyƒÅr), and only one of them was a Nambimar (Vaikhanasa priest) (TT. No. 67) (5) 1390 The Vaikhanasas have been an ancient and distinct religious community, long before the Sri-vaishnava group was formed under the leadership of N√§tha-muni (824-924 A.D.), Yamuna (died 1030 A.D.) and Ramanuja (1017-1137). It is likely that the worship in some of the shrines which the Alvars visited was conducted by the Vaikhanasa priests; the ideology of the first four Alvars (Poygai, Pey, 50 The Hill Shrine Bhuta and Tirumalisai) has strong correspondances with the Vaikhanasa outlook. Worship of Vishnu had become popular in the South by the time the Gangas began to rule (early fourth century). The Halmidi inscription (of about 450 A.D.), which is one of the earliest inscriptions, suggests the prevalence of the Vaikhanasa doctrine of Lakshmi-vi≈°ishtadvaita. ‡§ú‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç‡§ó‡§∂‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡§É‡•§ ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§µ‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§£‡•ã‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ó‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§É ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Å ‡§∏‡•Å‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§®‡§É ‡•§ The Vaishnava movement in the south gathered momentum when the Pallavas crossed the Vindhyas and came down to the South to establish a great empire here. The early Pallavas were Vaishnavas by creed and were ’temple-builders’ (chettha-kari). Mahendra-varman (580-630 A.D.) built the famous Mahendra-Vishnu-g·πõha in Mahendra-va·∏çi and the Vishnu caveshrine in Mamandur. His successors were responsible for Narasimha cavetemple at Singap-perumal-k≈çil, Ranganatha shrine at Singavaram and, Maha-varaha shrine at Mamallapuram. Of the early Chalukyas of Badami, ‘Mangalisa (596-619 A.D.) called himself a ‘parama-bhagavata’. The Alvars moved about and composed their pasuram-songs during the same period. The Vaikhanasa group was probably formed in the wake of the popularity of iconic worship and the institution of temples. The Vaikhanasas are a small community of temple priests, devoted The Hill Shrine to the worship of Vishnu in iconic form (samurtarchana). They trace their descent from an early group of hermits, who were within the Vedic fold and who combined sacrificial rituals with an ascetic and austere way of life. The worship of Vishnu was, according to them, tantamount to the performance of a great Vedic sacrifice; and Vishnu was for them the Vedic god par excellence. In fact, the credit for having institutionalized the iconic worship of Vishnu within the Vedic framework must go to the Vaikhanasas. They do not recognize Ramanuja or the earlier Alvars in their guru-prampara. Nor do they subscribe entirely to the Visishtadvaita doctrine as followed by the Sri-vaishnavas. They have their own philosophical position which is ably summarized in Srinivasamakhin’s Lakshmi-visishtadvaita- bhashya, based on the Vaikhanasa scriptures. They carry on their worship entirely with Vedic mantras and rituals, and have no place in their worship for the recital of Tamil prabandhas and tantrik ceremonials. They are strict in their adherence to the Vedic sacraments, and they also insist on the performance of fire-rituals, at home and in the temple, as ordained by the Taittiriya-brahmana texts. Equal importance is given by them to the Vedic sacraments and rituals on the one hand and worship of Vishnu-icon at home. and in temples on the other; in their view, the two are complementary to each other. They worship Vishnu alone 51as the supreme god comprehending in himself all other gods: ‡§Ö‡§•‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡•å ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§π‡•ã‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§£‡•ã‡§∞‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ö‡§æ ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ö‡§æ ‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ã ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§£‡•Å‡§É ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§¶‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡•á‡§£ ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡§æ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§Æ‡•ç‡•• Vaikhanasa-smarta-sutra, 4,10, 95 and 97) ‡§Ü‡§ñ‡§ø‡§≤‡§ú‡§ó‡§¶‡•á‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§£‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡§£‡•à‡§ï‡§™‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡•§ (Dasa-vidha-hetu-nirpa·πáa, 9) This is characteristically different from the Pancharatra view that the role of Lakshmi merits equal recognition. The Vaikhanasas, while accepting one Vishnu-icon as the main object of worship (mula or dhruva-bera) recognize four other iconic forms thereof for various ritual sequences: kautuka, snapana, utsava and bali. This is known as the ‘pa√±cha-murti’ ideology, quite distinct from the Vyuha doctrine of the Pancharatra. The main icon is likened to the fire that is worshipped at home (aupasanagni), and the other iconic forms are said to represent other ritual-fires that are derived and sanctified from this fire (sabhya, ahavaniya, anvaharya and avasathya). The five forms are technically called Vishnu, Purusha, Satya, Achyuta and Aniruddha. If Vishnu is the ‘prana’, the other four are apana, vyana, udana and samana. samana. (Marichi-VimƒÅnƒÅrchana- kalpa, patala 89). We will have octo deal with the five casion later iconic forms found in the hill-shrine. The Telugu-speaking Vaikhanasa priests appear to have been the original inhabitants of Tonda-mandala; and most of the temples in this area had Vaikhanasa priests. Later, they spread to the other areas, but after the advent of Ramanuja, they suffered a set-back, as he preferred the Pancharatra mode of worship. There are even today many important Vishnu temples in the South besides the hill-shrine, where worship is conducted according to VaikhƒÅnasa prescription; temples in Tirupati, Mamallapuram, Sri-Kakulam, Vijayanagaram, Amaravati, Tirucchirapalli, Vedadri, Dharmavaram, Kakinada, Chola-simha-puram, Ramanatha-puram, Pithapuram, Vinu konda, Khadri, Uttara-merur, Tanjavur, Tirukkurungu·∏çi, Madurai, Darbha Konda-vidu, Nellur, Tirumasai, Se√±ji, Vellur, Venkata-giri and Karve»õi-nagar. It is to be noted that many of the shrines intimately associated with Ramanuja and the Alvars (like those in Sri-perumbudur, Poonamalli, Vanama-malai, Alagar-k≈çil and Tiruvali-tirunagari) continue have Vaikhanasa priests, even as the hill-shine. The large-hearted Ramanuja does not seem to have cared to interfere with the age-old customs prevailing in these temples. sayanam, to 52 The Hill Shrine nenu Dove Ipa Gunka 3426 Akosa-dongo Theertham Wappa Theertham Chakra Tirtham 4140 Gadi Montopom atram attiram-bava Samadhi Parvatamonfopam TIRUMALAL Math Murla-gunfd pet Thirtham Kokula Konda Gogorbham Thirtham 29.09 1100 Vaikun Avochari Bashikal Mantapam -2240 TIRUMALA HILLS TOWN MAP NOT TO SCALE Year 1990 19 22 6 The Hill Shrine C6 " C4 24 19 (13 M 16 IR ÂêïÂêï 23 20 O 100 53

Known as ’tirtha-yatra’, it is regarded as a sacred duty and has been indulged in by our people since very ancient times. This has contributed considerably to the cultural unity of our population. Places thrown widely apart like Kedarnath and Badari in the Himalayas, Ramesvaram, and Kanyakumari in the extreme South, Dvaraka in the West, Puri-Jagannatha in the East; places like VƒÅra·πáƒÅsi, Prayaga, Vindhya-vasini, Gaya, Ujjayini, Nasik, Sri-saila, Madurai, Tanjavur, Kanchipuram, and U·∏çupi on the mainland, are well-known as places of pilgrimage. Among the large number of such pilgrim-spots, Tirumala-Tirupati has a unique place of honour. There are devotees of this shrine all over the country, and the shrine has been one of the most popular centres of religious devotion and piety since at least the beginning of the Christian era. A report included in the Meckenzie Manuscripts (Translations and Reports, Vol XVI, pages 491-493) dated 1804 A.D. has the following to say; (my comments are given within brackets): “1. Tirupathy (meaning TirumalaTirupati) is revered as a Mahachatra (viz mahƒÅ-kshetra, great place of pilgrimage) or very holy place, and many from various dasums (viz. desams, countries or rather parts of the country) resort thither, viz. 1. Cunnud (Karnataka), 2. Cannada (the coastal Karnataka) 3. Goojur (Gurjara, Gujarat) 4. Mahurvaada (Marwar, Rajasthan) 5. Maharastra 6. Sourastra (Saurashtra) 7. Conkan (Konkana, now included in Maharashtra) 8. Mallayal (Malayala or Kerala). Also Hindus come to visit the Swamee (viz. the hill-god) from several other countries (viz. parts of India) 2. Bellary etc” Tarigonda-VenkamƒÅmbƒÅ, a pious poetess who was a devotee of the hill-shrine, wrote in a work dated about 1750 that the hill-god receives offerings from pilgrims who come from a variety of places like Magadha, Malava, Malayala, Chola, Nepala, Konka·πáa, Dravida, Tenka·πáa, Saurashtra, Sauvira, Kuru, Kukara, Kosala, Pulinda, LƒÅta, MarƒÅta, Ghota, Karnata, Kamboja, Kashmira etc. (“Kasmiradi-sakaladesadhi≈õvaradhina-samasta-jana-vittƒÅpahara·πáam sƒÅdhu-satkara·πáam ≈õrivenkatesam”). People who visit the hill-shrine are all pilgrims, and few ever think of settling down there. And many of them go there to discharge a vow, to express their gratitude for a favour conferred, or to seek providential intervention in a predicament; and, therefore, they follow a regimen in order to make the pilgrimage effective and fruitful. There is an injunction that one who sets out on a pilgrimage must have faith and devotion; ‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä‡§§ ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§≠‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∏‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§§:
