Gooty 56 For, going on a pilgrimage, one can purify onself. ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•Å‡§ß‡•ç‡§¶‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ The Hill Shrine One must have good company (satsangi) while on a pilgrimage, must listen to good talk (satkatham ≈õ·πõ·πávan), and observe the codes of good conduct (sadachara-samanvitah). He must also be conversant with the details of how the particular pilgrimage is to be undertaken (vidhi-j√±ah): what spots are sacred and must be visited, what route in going round is proper, and how one should conduct himself while he is there. Texts like Brahma-purana (ch. 9 verses 3 ff) say that a pilgrimage helps understand the import of the scriptures. ‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§∂‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§ö‡•§ Unless one recognizes the veracity of scriptural prescriptions and teachings, he does not develop lasting faith, and it is such faith that brings about real devotion, which is indispensable for obtaining the ultimate benefit of pilgrimage (viz. grace of god). ‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§∂‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•á ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡•§ ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ø‡§§‡•á ‡§≠‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§§‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡•á‡§∂‡§µ‡§É‡•§‡•§ With regard to the pilgrimage undertaken to the hill-shrine, there are several details given in the purƒÅ≈Üas. The several sacred spots (tirthas) on the hill are mentioned; bath in tanks like SvƒÅmi-pushkarini and rivers like Svarna-mukhi is recommended; and approaches to numerous shrines are described. There is, however, a long poem in Sanskrit entitled Sri-venkatesaseva-krama, composed by GargyaSathari of the Tirupati village, which provides a graphic account of the main temple-complex on the hill, the ascent up the hill, enclosures and pavilions, deities and minor shrines along the circumambulatory paths. There is in it an excellent pen-picture of the hill-god. The exact date of composition of this work cannot be ascertained, but the pen-picture of the temple given here suggests that the work belongs to the fifteenth century. This work which has been included in Sri Venkatesa-Kavya Kalapa (ed. by D.T. TƒÅtacharya, T.T.D, Tirupati, 1943), is here given in full to focuss attention on the architecture of the temple. || ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡•á‡§∂‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§É ‡•• ‡§Ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•á ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡•á‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§æ‡§†‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø‡§ó‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•á ‡§ó‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§ø‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ This laudatory verse (taniyan) has been composed by a disciple of the author, who is mentioned as a resident of Tirupati. ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡§æ‡§ö‡§≤‡§æ‡§ß‡•Ä‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡§ø‡§§‡§µ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§ö‡•á‡§§‡§®‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§π‡§Ç ‡§≠‡§ú‡•á || ‡•ß‡•• The opening verse is in the nature of The Hill Shrine 57 as supplication to the hill-god who is addressed here Srinivasa and described as the lord of the Vengadam hill; Lakshmi abiding in his chest and the god being the protector of the people who seek refuge in him are given as his distinguishing features. ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§™‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡•á‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ó‡•Å‡§∞‡•ã‡§É ‡§™‡§¶‡•á ‡•§ ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡•á‡§∂‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§¨‡•ç‡§ú‡•á ‡§á‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡§æ‡§ö‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç || ‡•® || The devotee, desirous of making a pilgrimage to the Vengadam hill, should get up early in the morning and contemplate on the feet of his religious preceptor as also the sacred lotus-like feet of the lord of the Venga·∏çam hill. ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•á ‡§§‡§§‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§π‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§ï‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§É ‡•§ ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡§æ‡§ß‡•Ä‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡•á‡§Ø‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§®‡§≠‡•Ç‡§ß‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç ||‡•©|| Having left the town (Tirupati), he goes to the Chakra-tirtha for his bath and morning rites, before he approaches the sacred hill. KAPILA-TIRTHA The Chakra-tirtha mentioned here is more familiarly known as Kapila-tirtha, for there is a small shrine to Kapilesvara just beneath a boulder and on the banks of a pool formed by a splendid water-fall at the foot of the hill, about a mile to the north of the Tirupati town. This is a shrine to Siva constructed in the latter part of the eleventh century by Rayan-RajendraCholan (Brahma-mƒÅrƒÅyan), who was governing the village Kottur (which was on the outskirts of the Tirupati town and has now disappeared). The sanctum containing the linga called Kapilesvara is 6 3/4 x 7 1/4 x 7 cubic feet. To the north of the sanctum is another room 9 x 5 1/4 x 6 cubic feet, where the processional images are kept, along with the celestial cow of plenty, Kamadhenu, and its calf. In front of the sanctum is a porch (mukha-mandapa), where the bull sits facing the lingam in the sanctum. There are sculptural representations (facing west) of Uma-mahesvara (in the northern wing), Surya-nƒÅrƒÅya·πáa and Vinayaka (Southern wing). There are the usual door-guardians in the outer apartment. This tirtham (water-fall and pool) was used for the festival (like tirthavari) in the Govinda-raja temple at Tirupati, until Govinda-pushkari≈Üi was formed near the temple. RƒÅmƒÅnuja, who consecrated the Govindaraja temple is said to have installed the Sudarsana-chakra in the four directions on the banks of this tirtham. From this circumstance, it came to be known as Chakra-tirtha among the Sri-vaishnavas. The Tamil name for Sudarsana-chakra being Tiruvali-alvar, it was also known as Alvar-tirtha. There is also a shrine to Nammalvar on the southern side of the tank. 58 The Hill Shrine Entrance to Kapila-Tirtha But soon, the Sri-vaishnavas of Tirupati found little use for this tank, which is situated so far away; and it was neglected. The Vijayanagar monarch Achyuta-raya turned his attention to this tirtham, which he referred to in an inscription only as Chakra-tirtha, in 1531. The water-fall was rendered picturesque by granite stone steps; he got sandhya-vandanamantapas constructed in two rows on the eastern and western sides for the performance of ablutions and rituals by the pilgrim before he ascended the hill. This has been mentioned in the above verse (‘krta-purvƒÅhnika-kriyah’). Because of the presence of Kottur village and the growing popularity of The Hill Shrine Kapila-Tirtha (belore renovation) 59Kapila-Tirtha (after renovation) Tirupati from the twelfth century, the ascent to the Vengadam from Tirupati became more popular than the other approaches (viz., from Chandragiri and from Bale-palli). And from Tirupati, of the two alternative approaches, the road from the Municipal boundary of the town to Kapila-tirtha was preferred by most pilgrims. ‡§§‡§§‡§É ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ó‡•ç‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•á ‡§ó‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§™‡§•‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡§ó‡§ø‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§≤‡•á ‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∂‡•á‡§®‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§≠‡•Å‡§µ‡§É || ‡•™|| ‡§ö‡§ø‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•Å‡§≠‡§ó‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§≤‡§§‡§É ‡§∂‡•Å‡§≠‡•á‡•§ ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡•ã‡§ú‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ï‡§≤‡•ç‡§™‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•Å‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡•á ||‡•´|| ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§≤‡§§‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ã‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§æ‡§®‡§™‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§≤‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§®‡•É‡§∏‡§ø‡§Ç‡§π‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§ø‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡§ø‡§É || ‡•¨ || From Chakra-tirtha (viz. Kapila-tirtha) the pilgrim walks for some distance in the western direction along the wide pathway and comes to the foot of the Vengadam hills. There he sees the holy tamarind tree, looking splendid with spreading branches, and emanated from an aspect of Sesha (viz. Ananta, an attendant deity of Vishnu). Then he sights the holy feet (imprints) of the hill-god, appearing there from the god’s own choice. The pilgrim then visits the shrines of the Alvars, Poygai and others, prostrates before Lakshmi-Nrsimha and bows to the sacred hill (Seshadri). BEGINNING OF THE ASCENT Outside Kapila-tirtha are some interesting spots, like the cave-shrine of 60 The Hill Shrine The Hill Shrine 6 THE ASCENT ON THE HILL 61 Talayeru-Gundu Lakshmi-nrsimha to the west of the western bank of the tank, and the Nammalvar shrine at the entrance of the tank. The latter was built by Vanedutta-kai-alagiriyar (Pallava-rayar) about 1285 A.D. (vol I, No. 57, 58, 165). On the eastern road to the tank there is a shrine to Tirumangai-alvar, and to the west of the road leading to the tank a shrine to Kula-sekharaalvar, built by Ayodhya RƒÅmƒÅnuja jiyanagar. From Kapila-tirtha one walks in the westerly direction and reaches the flight of steps up the hill. The mantapa at this spot is called Alipiri-padala mar tapam (or Adi-vara-mantapam) :‘ali piri’ (adi-padi) means the lowest In the flight of steps at the foot of the hill. 62 Sri-Lakshmi-Narasimha-Svami temple (now deserted) The Hill Shrine The Hill Shrine Gali-Gopura (Agra-gopura, 1628) 63 64 Alipiri-Gopura Aliniri-opura, Padala Mandapam The Hill Shrine The Tamarind Tree The stone mantapam has a tower called Alipiri-gopuram (or Adipadagopuram). And not far from this mantapam are ancient shrines to the Periyalvar, Tiruppa·πáalvar and Tondar adip-pody-alvar. Nearby is the rock called Talayƒìru gundu, touch of which is said to bestow luck in ascending the hill; there is on this rock a splendid sculpture in relief of Hanuman. There is also to be seen in the same spot the famous tamarind tree, under which RƒÅmƒÅnuja is believed to have learnt mystic aspects of RƒÅmƒÅya·πáa from Tirumalanambi, his maternal uncle. RƒÅmƒÅnuja The Hill Shrine is regarded as an incarnation of Sesha, and this Vishnu’s couch, tamarind tree is said to share this detail. The story is that Ramanuja, before he became an Acharya, was residing for a year in Kottur, a village on the outskirts of the presentday Tirupati, for learning the secret doctrines taught in Ramayana from his uncle Tirumalanambi, who was residing on top of the hill, engaged in the daily service of the hill-god. Every morning Tirumala-nambi was coming down the hill and Ramanuja would be waiting for him under this tamarind tree. He would return as soon as the lessons were over, to be in time for the service of the hill-god. Out of consideration for this devotee, the hill-god manifested his holy feet close to the tamarind tree, so that Tirumala-nambi may offer his services to them, without having to hurry back. The feet of the hill-god are even now shown to the pilgrims, as engraved on a granite-slab in relief on the floor of what is known as Sri-vari- This p√§dala-mantapam. has been referred to in the above verses as ‘Srinivasa-padambhoje sva-samkalpƒÅt- samutthite’. There is also a shrine to Lakshmi n·πõsimha on the pathway to Seshadri (the name of the hill). This was constructed in 1485 by Saluva Nara simha to commemmorate his becoming the Vijayanagar monarch (sriman-sar- 65 vabhauma-narasimha) (G.T. No. 273, and No. 82). An inscription of 1544 refers to this temple as ’tirumalai-valikatti-singar koil’. This is said to have been destroyed by Hyder-ali when he attacked Chandragiri in 1782. ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∂‡§§‡§ï‡§Æ‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ö‡•ç‡§ö‡•à‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ó‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ö‡•ç‡§ö‡§∞‡§®‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§≠‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡§µ‡§§‡•à‡§É ‡§∏‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ã‡§π‡•á‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•É‡§∑‡§æ‡§ö‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç ||‡•≠| ‡§ò‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ò‡§∂‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡•ã‡§ö‡•ç‡§ö‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡•á‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•á‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É ‡•§ ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç ||‡•Æ|| ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£ ‡§≤‡§¨‡•ç‡§ß‡§ï‡§æ‡§Æ‡•à‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§®‡§∞‡•à‡§É ‡•§ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§ñ‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§ö ‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§ù‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§≠‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç ||‡•Ø|| ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡•á‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§™‡§•‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§Ü‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ã‡§¶‡§æ‡§¶‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•ã‡§µ‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç ||‡•ß‡•¶|| AS ONE ASCENDS complete, and watch the zealous devotees that are coming down with their wishes granted or going up to secure them. They are all oblivious of the difficulties and dangers of travel here. Devotees who resort to this hill are desirous of wealth, worldly pleasures, virtue or salvation. The first of stone steps beginning with Alipiri (Adi-padi) appears to have been renovated by Matla Ananta-raja (Kumara-Ananta), a chief of the Devachola family, exercising authority over the region between Tirupati and Ahobilam. The Agra-gopura (also known as Gali-gopuram) on the top of the first hill, which one reaches after climbing this winding flight of steps, was also constructed by him around 1628 A.D. A lamp was seen burning in this gopura at nights. And as one climbs the stone steps and walks along the foot-paths on the hill-tops, he is bewitched by the spectacle of lovely hills, dales, valleys, streams and thick jungles. It is of an unforgettable experience, and the pilgrim is not in a hurry: he rests at times, tarries on the steps or on the path, to drink deep the scenic beauty, and moves along in slow strides, at this stage of the journey. These verses describe the crowds that one meets with while ascending the hill. They constantly course constantly shout ‘Govinda’, not only to offset the strain of climbing the steep steps, but out of devotional fervour. Sri-vaishnavas would recite verses from Daya-sataka, a work of Vedanta-desika eulogizing the hill-god’s mercy, Yamuna-muni’s Stotra-ratna, or Ramanuja’s two works in prose: Saranagati-gadya and Vaikuntha-gadya. One should go along with devotees and learned men, so that the benefits of the pilgrimage may be 66 The ‘maha-patha’ mentioned here may probably refer to what now is known as ‘pedda-yekku·∏çu’, which is a very tiresome patch on the ascent. ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡§æ‡§ö‡§≤‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§™‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§™‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡•§ The Hill Shrine ‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡•Ä‡§ï‡•Ç‡§™‡§§‡§ü‡§æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§µ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§™‡§µ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§ö ‡•§‡•§ ‡•ß‡•ß ‡•§‡•§ ‡§∏‡•Å‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§∞‡•Ä ‡§ö‡•à‡§µ ‡§∂‡•ã‡§≠‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§ß‡§æ‡§Æ ‡§ó‡•ã‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Æ‡§™‡§ø ‡•§ | ‡•ß‡•® || ‡§§‡§§‡§É ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•Å‡§∞‡§≠‡§ø‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§ù‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§ó‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§§‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•É‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•Å‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§‡•§ ‡•ß‡•© ‡•§‡•§ Standing on the first hill-top, the pilgrim may turn round and see the land that he has covered, the stretch of land from the foot of the hills to the town of Tirupati of Tirupati (called here Sri-puri). Numerous tanks, pools, wells, streams, gardens and groves present themselves before him. He can see the meandering river Suvarna-mukhari, which flows past Tiruchanur and Chandra-giri and at a distance of a little more than two miles south of Tirupati. The pura·πáas describe this river as most sacred, and it is by the side of this river that the Vengadam hill rises, according to the purƒÅ≈Üas. It was an important river during the reigns of Pallavas and Cholas. He can also behold the great temple to Govinda-raja in the town. RƒÅmƒÅnuja on his second visit to this place found an ancient shrine to Parthasarathi on the outskirts of the village Kottur. As at that time there was a threat of desecration of the Srivaishnava temple at Chidambaram, the processional deity of Govinda-raja of that temple had to shifted to some safe place. be Ramanuja arranged for this deity to be housed within the Parthasarathi shrine near Kottur. Later, when the processional image was taken back to Chidambaram, and due to some reason the original icon of Parthasarathi could no longer be worshipped, Ramanuja installed a new main icon (mula-bera) here, and called it GovindarƒÅja. The processional image of Parthasarathi became the kautuka-bera of Govindaraja. This was in the year 1129-30 A.D. Ramanuja was also responsible for the new township called Tirupati, incorporating the area former ly known as Kottur. The YadavarƒÅya ruler Vira-narasinga-deva contributed to the magnificence of this temple and Pedda-Yekkudu The Hill Shrine 67 the celebrity of this temple-town ( around 1234 A.D.) The ’tirtha’ mentioned in verse 13 is actually a canal that one comes to, after the first steep ascent and before the steep flight of steps known as ‘knee-breaker’ (monakal murippu). ||‡•®‡•©|| NEARING THE SUMMIT Then follows a graphic description of the charming wilderness that abounds on the hills. The pilgrim looks at the tall trees of many varieties, listens to the sounds of many birds and beasts, and enjoys the cool and fragrant breeze that wafts across. Many are the animals that freely roam about in the forests on the hills, and they seem to live amicably. The pilgrim, filled with devotion, imagines that the fortunate birds and animals that live on these sacred hills are indeed the celestial beings ( nitya-suris) who have assumed those forms. The nitya-suris are the ever-liberated beings who have secured fully the grace of the great god, but they are eager to dwell on these hills and forests of Vengadam, which is verily Vaikuntha where they normally reside. They, like the god himself, have abandoned Vaikuntha and have teamed up here to be near the god. The journey to the hill-shrine is accomplished by ascents and descents over hills (which are seven in number) and moving in and out of forests. The landscape is throughout picturesque and delightful. There is never a dull moment for the pilgrim as he walks amidst trees laden with fruits and flowers, plants and creepers which are shapely and charming, and scents and sounds which are fascinating. But now he is all eager to get near the hill-shrine, and hurries onward. 68 The Hill Shrine ‡§§‡§§‡•ã‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•É‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§§‡•ã‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ö‡•§ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡§ö‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§®‡•ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§®‡•ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡§ö‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§™- ‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç || ‡•®‡•™|| ‡§∏‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ã‡§π‡§®‡•ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∂ ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§µ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•Å‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§¶‡§æ || ‡•®‡•´ || ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•Å‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§§‡§É ‡§™‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§É ‡•§ affermayer fact ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•á‡§ß‡§∏‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§‡•§ ‡•®‡•¨ ‡•§‡•§ ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡§™‡§Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§µ‡§¶‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§™‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•å‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ ‡•®‡•≠ ‡•§‡•§ He has to climb one hill and get down, only to climb another. He moves on, resting for a while, and contemplating on the god whose presence he is approaching. He reaches the final hill, and as he climbs it, the thought uppermost in his mind is that the god should draw him soon to his presence. DOVA-BHƒÄSHYAKƒÄRA-SANNIDHI When he climbs the last flight of steps rightly called the ‘knee-breaker’ (molakal-murippan-pa·∏çi or mokalamettu), for the steps are so high and the ascent so steep, he comes to the top of the hill that is chosen by the god for his manifestation. Even as he reaches the summit of the flight of steps, he finds himself in front of the shrine dedicated to Ramanuja. This shrine is known as Dova-Bhashyakarasannidhi, and marks the commencement of the holy area of Vengadam. The pilgrim has at last entered the sacred precincts of the extended hillshrine. There is an ancient custom of lighting a lamp in this D≈çvaBhashyakara-sannidhi by the temple authorities on Kartika-dipotsava, marking one of the outer limits of the kshetra, the other one being the Kshetra-palaka shrine near Gogarbhatirtha. When RƒÅmƒÅnuja visited Tirupati for the first time to learn the esoteric meanings of Ramayana from his maternal uncle Tirumala-nambi, he was still a young man whose greatness was not as yet recognized. He did not, at that time, ascend the Vengadam hill to visit the hill-shrine, although he stayed at the foot-hills for a year. But the second time he visited Tirupati he was widely revered Acharya duly anointed in the great temple of Sri-rangam. This time, he ascended the hills and as soon as he climbed up the last flight of steps, his maternal uncle, Tirumalanambi, received him with all templehonours at the spot where the shrine (known as Dova-Bhashyakara-sannidhi) was later erected. Beyond this shrine, the poem speaks of a long row of fine mantapams on either side of the pathway that led the pilgrim to the hill-shrine. They are not all there now, nor are those that remain so fine. As the pilgrim enters the sacred precincts of the kshetra, he is overpowered by the excitement and joy of The Hill Shrine 6970 Mondi-Gopuram (now dismantled) Bommala-Gopuram (now dismantled) The Hill Shrine those who have already reached there. An air of festivity surrounds him. Music fills his ears. The mantapas as well as the ground alround are decorated with flowers of variegated colours; and the sight is delightful to his eyes. As he walks along, bewitched by the beauty of the place, he beholds a small tower, bereft of the usual vase and finial (≈õrnga-vihinam tad-gopuram). The reference is to the unfinished gateway, surmounted by a tower facing south, called Mondi-gopuram. As we proceed from this Mondi-gopuram towards north through a short pathway, we come to another tower called Bommala-gopuram. Both these ornate gopurams were dismantled in 1982 to make way for a plain cement-arch which appeared three years later. The ten incarnations of Vishnu were prominently displayed here. Passing through this gateway, the pilgrim enters the village where the hill-shrine is located. In fact, he has now the first vision of the sacred temple. ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Å‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•à‡§∞‡•Å‡§ú‡•ç‡§ú‡•ç‡§µ‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§§‡§§‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡•ã‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∂‡•Å‡§∂‡§§‡§ï‡•ã‡§ü‡§ø‡§∏‡§Æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§≠‡§Æ‡•ç ||‡•©‡•¨|| ‡§Æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡§ó‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§®‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ã‡§∑‡§≠‡•Ç‡§≠‡•É‡§§‡§É ‡•§ ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§µ‡§®‡•Å‡§™‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§ï‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§æ||‡•©‡•≠|| ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§®‡§ø‡§≤‡§Ø‡§æ‡§π‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§®‡§ó‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§µ‡•à‡§ï‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§†‡§Æ‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç ||‡•©‡•Æ|| ‡§ß‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•Å‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡•Ä‡§§‡•ã‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ã‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡•§ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§≠‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§ß‡•Ä: ‡•§‡•§ ‡•©‡•Ø ‡•§‡•§ His dream of many days, months or years has now come true; he stands in the close vicinity of the most sacred of all shrines. He can see the temple at a distance and over the sanctum shines brilliantly the dome with its gilt covering. The vimana, known as Ananda-nilaya, is believed by the faithful to have appeared here of its own accord (svayam-vyakta) for securing the welfare of the world (lokƒÅnugraha). It is indeed a unique structure on earth, a bright ornament on the head of the Vengadam hill (Seshadri). The Hill Shrine Mokalia-Mudippu 71 The pilgrim moves on in the village, which indeed is like another Vaikuntha, congratulating himself on his own good fortune in having been able to come here. He is grateful to the spirits who have made his ascent of the hill safe and memorable; and he is full of praise for the god whom he has come to see. ANANDA-NILAYA-VIMƒÄNA This is undoubtedly the most picturesque and attractive structure in the temple-complex. The golden plates covering it are intricately and exquisitely carved with traditional motifs. The shape of the Vimana very strongly resembles the Dharma-raja-ratha at Mamallapuram (which was built by the Pallava monarch Dantivarma of Kanchi puram in 830 A.D.). It is very likely that this vimana in the hill-shrine is also a Pallava structure. The height of the vimana is 37 feet and 8 inches, including the ceremonial vase on the top (kalasa). It has a square base, measuring twenty-seven feet and four inches. The tower is divided into three sections, the bottom one being square and having no figures; the middle one being rectangular in shape and having nineteen figures. The figures are all sculptures in relief and the themes are the incarnations of Vishnu, the door-guardians, attendents (like Garuda, Vishvaksena and Hanuman) and the sages. In the four directions are the customary forms of Vishnu described as the presiding deities of the vimana (vimanadhi-daivata): Varaha, Narasimha, Ananta and Vaikuntha-natha. On the northern side is the representation of the deity in the sanctum, known as Vimana-Srinivasa. It is said that Vyasatirtha used to worship this small iconic representation. It has become customary to have a look at this figure (Vimana-Srinivasa) while going round the shrine in Vimana-pradakshina. The figures and the ornamental decorations of this Vimana are only in the gilt copper plates that cover the vimana. The actual vimana which is in mortar and stucco is devoid of figures; it has only the architectural features of the Pallava vimana. We do not know how old the vimana is. But we have inscriptional references to the covering of the vimana with copper plates gilt by the Yadava chieftain Vira-narasinga-deva in 1260 A.D. and the fixing of a golden vase (kalasa) on the vimana by the Pandyan king Ja·π≠ƒÅ-varma Sundara-pandya in 1262 A.D. (T.T. 371 No. 19). The Yadava chieftain was a feudatory of the Pandyan king, and the two together made the vimana look like the mythical mountain of gold (Sumeru, sura-giri) The next service to the vimana was in the year 1359 A.D. (6-7-1959), Saluva Mangi-deva-mahƒÅrƒÅja, who was the general under Kumara-kampa≈Üaodeyar is said to have fixed the golden sikhara for Tiru-vengala-natha’, on that 72 The Hill Shrine date. The vimana was renovated in 1417 A.D. by Amatya-sekhara Mallana, who was the governor of Chandra-girirajya, during the rule of Saluvanarasimha (I, 198). The famous Vijayanagar monarch Krishnadeva-raya presented thirty thousand gold varahas for gilding the vimana again, during his fifth visit to the hill-shrine. The work began on 2-1-1517 and was completed on 9-9-1518 (III 81, TT 576). Koti-kanya-dƒÅnam Tatacharya of Kanchipuram got the vimana gilt again in 1630 (Madras Epigraphical Report 1919, 650). Adhikari Ram-lakshman Dasa, a brother-disciple of the then Mahant Prayaga-dƒÅsa, arranged for gold plating the vase over the vimana (kanaka-kalasa) on 30-9-1908. Thus, in seven hundred years (1261-1908) there were six services to (vimƒÅna-kainkarya) the vimana rendered. The gold plates covering the vimana soon lost their brilliance and the covering plates themselves got loosened and disturbed because of the weather conditions prevailing on the hill. After the period of the Mahants, the TTD took over the administration of the temple, and thought of goldplating the vimana again in 1958. It was then discovered that the masonry work of the vimana beneath the covering had decayed, and that it had to be repaired before the old plates were replaced by new ones. The work began with the Balalaya-ritual (October 25, to November 1, 1957), and the gold plates were set in position on November 2, 1957 (kavacha-punar-uddharana). Final consecration (mahasamprokshana) was performed on August 25, 1958. Copper sheets worth Rs 57, 375 and gold weighing 12, 020 tolas were used in this renovation. The puranas describe this vimƒÅna as Ananda-nilaya. But we find the name Ananda-nilaya-vimana for the first time in a damaged inscription of 1417 A.D. of Amatya-sekhara Mallana (No. 196). Bhavishyottara-purana (13, 80-81) says that even when the first builder of the temple, Tondaman, consecrated the vimana, it was called Ananda-nilaya, because it produced great delight. ‡§§‡§§ ‡§Ü‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§®‡§ø‡§≤‡§Ø‡•á ‡§§‡•ã‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡•É‡§™‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•á‡•§ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡•ã ‡§∞‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú ‡§≠‡§ó‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§π‡§∞‡§ø‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ü‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§ú‡§®‡§ï‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§Ü‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§®‡§ø‡§≤‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•Å‡§É ‡•§‡•§ Ananda-nilaya-vimana The Hill Shrine 73 Aditya-purana (1, 42) explains that the god is distinguished by his power to bestow bliss and gnosis and so he is himself known essentially as bliss (ananda); when Tondaman beheld him in this vimana, he was filled with bliss. ‡§Ü‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§£‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§Ü‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§Ü‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•á‡§® ‡§¶‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§æ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§Ü‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§®‡§ø‡§≤‡§Ø‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ø‡•á ‡•§‡•§ The pura·πáas also claim that when the hill-shrine was consecrated, Brahma the creator put up two lamps in front of the god for the alround prosperity of all beings in the world. And he begged the god to ordain that these lamps may be burning till the end of the present aeon (kali-yuga). When the Ananda-nilayavimana crumbles down and the lamps are put out, then the manifestation of the god as Venkatesa (lord abiding in the Vengadam hill) would be over. ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡§Ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ã ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§ï‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡•§ ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ ‡§µ‡•à ‡§≠‡§ó‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§™‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§ï‡§≤‡•ç‡§™‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏ ‡§ó‡•ã‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∞‡§Æ‡•á‡§ï‡§Æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§ï‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ó ‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§¶‡•Ä‡§™‡•ã‡§≠‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡•• ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§™‡§§‡§®‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§™‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§∂‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç || ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§É ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§Æ‡•á ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ã‡§µ‡§ß‡§ø: ‡•§‡•§ (Bhavishyottara, 14, 2-3) The pilgrim bows with delight before this most impressive indication of divine presence, and proceeds towards it. He now enters what is known as Sannidhi street, which runs east to west. 74 SANNIDHI-STREET ‡§§‡§§‡§É ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§ß‡§ø‡§µ‡•Ä‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§≠‡•Ç‡§∑‡§£‡§Ç ‡§™‡§µ‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ú‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§®‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§§‡§É ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡§Æ‡§£‡•ç‡§ü‡§™‡§Æ‡•ç ||‡•™‡•¶|| The Sannidhi-street is so called because it leads the pilgrim directly to the presence of the god. This short and rather narrow (until recently) street extended from the shrine to Anjaneya, known as Bƒì·∏çi-Hanuman, already mentioned, at the eastern end to the tall four-pillared and open mantapa (called Golladani-mantapa) at the Western end. Bƒì·∏çi-Hanuman faces west, and stands right in front of the main (and only) entrance to the hill-shrine, at the other end of the street. To the left of the street is the so called thousand-pillared mantapam, and to the right the Periya-jiyar matham, whose head is an important functionary in the hill-shrine. The street ends with Golladani-mantapam, which faces the gateway to the shrine. This mantapam, was formerly used for occasional festivals, but now is altogether unused. The Sannidhi street was widened in 1970 and it no longer presents the picture that once was familiar. The Bedi-Hanuman shrine has now been isolated, and it is almost a new structure now. Two bays of the thousand-pillared mantapa on the side The Hill Shrine The Hill Shrine 75 THE HILL-SHRINE 76 THE VIMANA The Hill Shrine 7 The Hill Shrine Sannidhi-Street 1970 Sannidhi-Street 1993 77 Thousand-Pillared Mantapa (western side) Now Dismantled of East Ma·∏ça-street were removed; and three bays of the same mantapam on the Sannidhi-street side were dismantled and shops were constructed. From Golladani-mantapa which marks the end of the Sannidhi-street, there is a flight of steps to the full width of the road (viz. Sannidhi-street). The pilgrim has to climb down this flight of steps to reach the main entrance of the temple which opens to the East Mada Street, running north to south. This flight of steps was renovated in 1990, and now it extends to the end of the East Mada-Street on the southern side, so that one can enter the thousand-pillared mantapa from the East-Mada street by climbing the flight of steps. The thousand-pillared mantapam which faces east is a rectangular structure, accommodated within the area covered by the Sannidhi-street on the north, East Mada-street on the west, a pathway on the east, and the road in front of Hathiramji-matha on the south. This mantapam was formerly used by the pilgrims, but now it houses the assembly hall (sadas), which in recent years was shifted to the Asthana-mantapam, Museum and Purohita-sangham. The western portion of the mantapam had crumbled down, leaving an open space on that side. 78 The Hill Shrine We do not know precisely when this mantapam was constructed, or by whom. It appears to have been one of the structures undertaken to give effect to an endowment of the Durgasamudram village (near Tirupati) to the hill-temple made by the Saluva Narasimha (in 1472 A. D), the governor of the Chandra-giri province who rose to become the lord of the entire Vijayanagar (‘rƒÅja-mahƒÅrƒÅja-udaiyar). This endowment was meant to enable mantapas and gopuras to be constructed in Tirumala and Tirupati. The name of Saluva-Mallaya-deva, son of SaluvaErra-Kampaya-deva, a cousin of Saluva-Narasimha, is associated with the building of this mantapa. It cannot be known if the original mantapa was designed to have a thousand pillars. The sahasra-stambha mantapa is a familiar architectural member of big temples in South India. It may not always have a thousand pillars. When the Sanskrit expressions ‘dasa’ (ten ), ‘sata’ (hundred ) and sahasra’ (thousand) are used in contexts like this, they are not meant to signify those specific numbers, but are used only in the sense of multitude. The ’thousand-pillared mantapa’ means only a hall with a large number of the pillars. ‡§≠‡•á‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Æ‡•É‡§¶‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§™‡§£‡§µ‡§§‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∂‡§ô‡•ç‡§ñ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§∂‡•Å‡§≠‡•à‡§É ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§¶‡§∂‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á- ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ò‡•ã‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡§®‡§≠‡§É ‡§∏‡§®‡§ï‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ||‡•™‡•´|| ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∏‡§∞‡•ã‡§ó‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§®‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ó‡•Ä‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§Ü‡§¨‡§æ‡§≤‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¨‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ï‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡§ø‡§∂‡•ã‡§≠‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§ó‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§®‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∂‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ||‡•™‡•≠|| ‡§∏‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•É‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•à‡§É ‡§∏‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∂‡•ã‡§≠‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡•á‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡•Ä‡§§‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§É || ‡•™‡•Æ|| The main entrance to the temple is described here. The temple doorway is decorated with festoons, garlands and buntings; fragrant smokes of incense fill the air. The doors are guarded by Chanda, Prachanda and other attendants mentioned in the Agama texts. Celestial beings crowd the entrance, as also the spiritual adepts in their invisible bodies. Pilgrims from different parts of the country, young and old, with their families are jostling about in front of the entrance. The Hill Shrine 79They are filled with devotion and bow down humbly with palms joined in supplication above their heads. They are all eager to enter. The text calls this entrance, which one encounters as soon as he gets down the flight of steps from the Sannidhi-street, ‘Vaikuntha-dvara’. The expression is now used for the entrance to the enclosure immediately around the sanctum (viz., Vaikunthapradakshina or Mukkoti-pradakshina) and which is kept open only once in a year (on the night of Mukkotiekadasi). The door-way that the text speaks of is not what is now called Pa·∏çi-kavali (entrance guarded by sentinels) also called Maha-dvara (the main entrance, tiru-vƒÅ≈õal in Tamil), for the text later on mentions (in verse 56) that the pilgrim enters the maha-dvara after going round the main streets of the village. And, according to the text, he ’enters’ (‘praviseyam’) this Vaikunthadvara to reach the main streets. What, or where, then is this Vaikuntha-dvƒÅra now? Was it some structure that no longer exists? or does it refer to what we now know as Golladani-mantapam at the end of the Sannidhi-street? It is hard to ascertain what the condition was at that time. It is likely that there was an ornamental arch-way at the foot of the flight of steps (from the Sannidhi-street) without either the prakara walls on the sides or the gopuram on its top. It was perhaps meant to indicate the entrance into ine temple-area, which was described by the devout as Vaikuntha itself. The verses included in the mantra-pushpa traditionally recited in this temple say that the god enjoys himself on the banks of the sacred tank here, having come away from Vaikuntha. And wherever the god dwells is verily Vaikuntha. ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡•Ä ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§µ‡•à‡§ï‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§†‡§Æ‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•á ‡§∞‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∏‡§π ‡§Æ‡•ã‡§¶‡§§‡•á ‡•§ ‡§≠‡•ã‡§ó‡§ø‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§ó‡§ø‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§ó‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§ø‡§§‡§ü‡•á‡•§ ‡§∞‡§Æ‡§§‡•á ‡§∞‡§Æ‡§£‡•Ä‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§∞‡§Æ‡§£‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§É ‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§É‡•§ Such an archway would allow the pilgrim to go round the streets of the village before entering the present Padi-kavali, which is situated just a few feet away, on the other side of the East-Ma·∏ça street. THE ENTRANCE TO THE SHRINE ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§≠‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∏‡§æ‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§®‡•Å‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡§æ‡§ß‡•Ä‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§§‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ areafer: 118911 ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§µ‡•Ä‡§•‡•Ä‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§£‡§™‡§∞‡•ã ‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ||‡•´‡•¶|| ‡§≠‡§µ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§π‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É 97:1 ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•á‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§®‡§æ‡§∂‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç ||‡•´‡•ß| 80 The Hill Shrine +10-6”> ‚Üê 16! ‚Üí‚Üí !‚Üê 9!67 I PRATIMA- I MANTAPA ‚Üê24.6-> „Åì < 16-9 -> The Hill Shrine < 8’ 2’ ŸàÿßŸà 4313” MAHA-DVƒÄRA 16-91 M 13‚Ä≤„Éº„Éê„Éº„É© ‚Üê 13-8-> 81 82 PADI-KAVALI Maha-Dvara The Hill Shrine The Hill Shrine Entrance to the Hill-Shrine Inner Prakaram 83 View of Tirumala Temple The pilgrim prostrates before the main entrance and begins the first great circumambulation (maha-pradakshina), which takes him round the village which is also holy. He turns to his left, keeping the shrine to his right, and proceeds along the East MƒÅ·∏çaStreet. Then he turns to his right and enters the South Mada-Street which goes by the side of the outer wall of the temple. He proceeds along this road beyond the temple another four hundred feet (the compound wall of the temple on this side is 414 feet) before he turns and enters the West Mada Street (which is about 900 feet in length). He would by now have covered the village proper, the Pata-pushkarini area 84 where a tank had been excavated by the Vijayanagar king, Achyuta-rƒÅya, in the sixteenth century, and which was filled up during the days of the Mahants in the nineteenth; and, at the far end of the road, the shorter side of the sacred tank (Svami-pushkari≈Üi) Before he gets back to the East Mada street he covers the entire length of the tank before he reaches the north-eastern corner of the temple, walking along this road. The south-eastern corner of the temple faces the Matha of the Mahants, on the raised ground on level with the top of the terrace of, and adjacent to, the thousand-pillared mantapam. It is learnt that one of the later The Hill Shrine Yadava-rƒÅya kings, Sri-ranganatha, built for himself a palace in 1351-52 A.D. on the hill here (I, 109). It was probably this palace that was appropriated by the Mahants who administered the temple as Vichara·πáa -kartas from 1843 to 1933. THE SECRED TANK SVƒÄMI-PUSHKARINI ‡§§‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•á ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§π‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§ï‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§É ‡§µ‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§ø‡§£‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§µ‡§∏‡•Å‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§‡•§‡•´‡•®‡•§‡•§ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡§ø‡§É ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§£‡§Æ‡§π‡§Ç ‡§§‡§§‡§É ‡•§ ‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§£‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§®‡•ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∂‡•à‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä‡§É ‡•§‡•§ ‡•´‡•© ‡•§‡•§ The pilgrim now comes to the sacred tank called Svami-pushkari≈Üi (or Svami-saras, or Tiruk-k≈çneri in inscriptions), ceremonially sips water from it and finishes his noon-ablutions there. Then he visits the Varaha-svami’s shrine which is on the banks of this tank, and circumambulates the shrine as well as the tank before proceeding towards the great temple which has not been mentioned by the Alvars at all, SvƒÅmi-pushkarini in the pura·πáas as most sacred upon the hill: is extolled. ‡§∏‡•Å‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§∞‡•Ä ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ ‡§®‡§¶‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§®‡§¶‡•Ä ‡•§ ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§è‡§µ‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡•á ‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•á ‡§µ‡•É‡§∑‡§≠‡§æ‡§ö‡§≤‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§§‡§É‡•§ ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡•á ‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§®‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä ‡§∂‡•Å‡§≠‡§æ ‡•§‡•§ (Skanda, 4, 1, 25, 86) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä ‡§ö‡•á‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§®‡•ã‡§¶‡§ø‡§®‡•Ä ‡•§ ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡•á ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡•á ‡§Æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§≤‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡§æ ‡•§‡•§ (ibid., 4, 1, 86) ‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä ‡§∂‡•Å‡§≠‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ (Brahma 8, 2) near The tank which is almost at the middle of the North Mada-Street is 100 yards in length and 50 yards in width, covering a total area of 1.50 acres. It is actually twin-tanks: Varaha-tirtha near the Varaha-svami shrine which is on its north-western shore, and ≈örinivasa-tirtha, the hill-shrine which is on its southern side. A low wall separates the two parts. And traditionally the large tank is said to consist of nine separate well-like water sources (tirthas), each displaying silt deposits of a particular colour. According to Brahma-purana (8, 32-38): i) on the northern side of the tank, Kubera-tirtha; ii) in the north-eastern corner, Galava tirtha; iii) on the eastern side, Markandeya- tirtha; iv) in the south-eastern corner, Agni- tirtha; The Hill Shrine 85 v) on the southern side, Yama-tirtha; vi) in the north-western corner, Vayu- tirtha; vii) on the western side, Varuna-tirtha; viii) in the south-western Vasishtha-tirtha, and corner, ix) in the centre, Sarasvati-tirtha. There are cut-stone steps for the tank alround, and from the midst of the waters rises a mantapa called Vasanta-ma·πá·π≠apa (or Nirali-mantapa), which was built by Saluva-narasimha in 1468 A.D. (TT No. 54). It is interesting that his inscription calls the tank not Svami-pushkari≈Üi but Tirukk≈çnƒìri, although another inscription thirty-five years earlier (I, 201, DevarƒÅya) calls it SvƒÅmi-pushkari≈Üi; and this is the first time the sacred tank comes to our notice in inscriptions. The Vasanta-mantapa was meant to be used for the floating (tiruppalli-o·∏çattirunal) on the ninth day of the annual festival, Brahmotsava (or K≈ç·∏çait-tirunal) This mantapa was renovated by Mahanta Prayaga-dasa in 1900; it would appear that the mantapa had almost crumbled down by that time. SvƒÅmi-pushkarini is an important landmark on the hills, for it is on the banks of this tank that the god of the hills (Venga·∏çattan) chose to manifest himself, according to the puranas. The legendary account of the origin of the hill-shrine relates that Varaha-svami (the boar-incarnation of Vishnu) was the original inhabitant of the land adjoining the tank; and that he allowed the god of the hills to dwell in a part of the land owned by him. And Brahma himself is said to have consecrated a shrine to Varaha on the north-western shore of the tank. It is related that Varaha hid himself in a huge ant-hill which, in fact, was his residence. ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§ø‡§µ‡•á‡§∂ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§µ‡§≤‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä‡§§‡§ü‡•á‡•§ (Brahma, 8, 14, 21) Later, Brahma consecrated a stoneimage of this form of Vishnu, which was dug out from the ant-hill by the hunter (nishƒÅda) who also constructed a shrine. ‡§®‡§æ‡§π‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø ‡§§‡•á ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§∏‡§ö‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§µ‡§ø‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§É ‡•§ ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Å ‡§µ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø ‡§§‡•á ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡•§‡•§ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§µ‡§≤‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§ï‡•á ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡•á ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§Æ‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§∞ ‡•§ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§ö ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä‡§§‡§ü‡•á ‡•§‡•§ The shrine and the part of the tank in front of it was named Varaha-tirtha: ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•á ‡§§‡•Å ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Æ‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ The custom prescribed for the pilgrim in the pura·πáas is that Varaha svƒÅmi must be worshipped after a bath in the sacred tank and before he proceeds to the hill-shrine. ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§®‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•á ‡§§‡•Å ‡§µ‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§: ‡•§ ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§´‡§≤‡§§‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡§ø ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡•ã ‡§® ‡§§‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡•§‡•§ 86 The Hill Shrine ADI-VARAHA-SHRINE ‡§ß‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ç‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§≤‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ü‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§™‡§ø‡§™‡•ç‡§™‡§≤‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§ø‡§§‡•á‡•§‡•§ The icon that we find in the shrine now is a small one with the four-armed Varaha, standing and lifting up the earth-goddess with two of his hands. It is in relief on a rock. The description of Adi-varaha given in the VaikhƒÅnasa text Vimanarchana-kalpa (56th patala) is fully portrayed in the main image here. ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡•Å‡§ú‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§Ç‡§ñ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§ß‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§®‡§ø‡§≠‡§Ç ‡§®‡§æ‡§ó‡•á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§´‡§£‡§æ‡§Æ‡§£‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡§¶‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§£‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ç acu The Varaha-shrine on the hill, however, is not mentioned by any of the Alvars, or in any inscription before 1380 A.D., when Alagap-piranar Tirukkalikan·πõ-dasar made an endowment to this small shrine. The deity is called here Varaha-nƒÅyanar. As mentioned earlier, the next inscription, eighty-six years later in 1476 A.D. (by Sathak≈çpar Narasimha-rayamudaliar) refers to this minor god on the hill as Jnanappiran, a typical Sri-vaishnava name (’tiruk-k≈çnƒìri karaiyil j√±ƒÅnappiran’). After 1481 he is referred to as Adi-varahap-perumal (Kanda·∏çe Dod-qucni g√†n tai ·∏çappayyangar, 1519, Tallapakam Tiru malayyangar, 1535, Settlur Srinivasayyangar, 1541). It was Tallapakam Tirumalayyangar who constructed the mantapas, the prƒÅkƒÅra-wall and the east gopura of this shrine in 1535 (IV 40). However, it has always remained a minor shrine, and when the East India Company came in to take control over the shrines on the hill, the annual income of this shrine was not more than Rs. 250. ‡§§‡§¶‡•Ç‡§∞‡•å ‡§Æ‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§£‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•á‡§® ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡•å ‡§ó‡•Å‡§π‡•ç‡§£‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç Bhavishyottara-purana (3, 10-11) provides a general iconographic description of Varaha: ‡§è‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§µ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä ‡§∂‡•Å‡§≠‡§æ ‡•§ ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ: ‡§¶‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§£‡§¶‡§ø‡§ó‡•ç‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•á ‡§µ‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡§µ‡§¶‡§®‡•ã ‡§π‡§∞‡§ø‡§É‡•§ ‡§ú‡§ø‡§ò‡•ç‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡•Ä‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§¨‡§∞‡§ß‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≠‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§°‡§æ‡§π‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§≠‡§∞‡§£‡§∏‡§Ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§®‡§æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§™‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§§‡§æ‡§≤‡•á‡§® ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡•ç The god must be represented as four-armed. Two of his hands (the upper ones) carry conch and discus. His complexion is grass-green! (dark). His right leg is placed on the hood of Adi-sesha and his left leg is bent for The Hill Shrine 87 ascending from the depths of the netherworld. He carries the goddess of earth on his thigh, supporting her feet by his right hand, and holding her up with his left. His head is turned towards the goddess, smelling her with his snout. The goddess, dark in com - plexion, folds her palms in supplication and looks at the god with coyness and glee. She is decorated with all ornaments, and her garment is flowery in design. She must be made in five tala-measure and her height must reach the god’s navel or breast. A similar but more concise description is given in Prakimadhikara (Bhargava-samhita) ‡§ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§¨‡§æ‡§π‡•Å‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§Ç‡§ñ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§ß‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ï‡•Å‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Æ‡§π‡•Ä‡§Æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§æ‡§¶‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ ‡§¶‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§£‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§π‡•Ä‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§∞‡•å ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ö‡§∞‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡•§ ‡§Æ‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Å ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ö‡•ç‡§ö‡•à‡§µ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡§§‡§É‡•§ ‡§è‡§µ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡•á‡§≤‡•ç‡§≤‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç‡•• ‡§ï‡•Å‡§≤‡§∂‡•á‡§ñ‡§∞‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä‡§∞‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ö‡•ç‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡•à ‡§∞‡§•‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É ‡§™‡§∞‡•Ä‡§§‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡•á‡§∂‡§Æ‡•Å‡§™‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ö ‡••‡•´‡•™‡•• ‡§®‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§π‡§ß‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§ß‡•Ä: ‡•§ ‡§Ü‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡§ö‡§ø‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ö‡§≤‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§∂‡§Ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§§‡§É ‡•§‡•§ ‡•´‡•´ ‡•§‡•§ Coming out of the Varaha-shrine, the pilgrim walks along the North Ma·∏çaStreet keeping the sacred tank 88 SRISWAR SAASRA BEERALAKAKARAM SE ‡§∏‡§æ‡§π‡§∏‡§¶ Koluvu Mandapam The Hill Shrine The Hill Shrine H’SP Mahadvara-Gopura 89SAM KHA-NIDHI (at Main Entrance) to his right, and enters the East Ma·∏ça-Street. Going beyond the length of the sacred tank, he beholds to his left the tall shed where the processional chariot (ratha) is kept. He bows to this chariot and proceeds towards the main entrance of the shrine. He returns to where he began his maha-pradakshina and enters the tall gateway known as Maha-dvara (or mukha-dvara; Periyatiru-vƒÅ≈õal), or Padi-kavali. The gateway to the shrine is 43’ 3” wide and about 30’ long. The passage through which one can enter is 9’ 9” wide, and is in the centre of this gateway. The passage widens when one reaches what is known as the Pratima-mantapam; it will be 16’ PADMA-NIDHI (at Main Entrance) here. On the large stone slabs covered with metal sheets constituting the door step are now seen two bronze icons of the traditional gateway-gods Samkha-nidhi and Padma-nidhi. Agama prescribes that these are the guardians of the third of the seven enclosures (avara·πáa) that may be had for a shrine. It may be noted that the hill-shrine has only three enclosures covered by Sampangi-pradakshina (outer), Vimana-pradakshina (middle) and Mukkoti-pradakrishna (inner). The Mahadvara (the principal gateway) admits the pilgrim to the outer (Sampangi) enclosure. On either side, two cut stone door-frames are to be seen. In the words of T.K.T. Viraraghava- 90 The Hill Shrine ‡∞µ‡±Ü‡∞≤‡±Å‡∞™‡∞≤‡∞ï‡±Å ‡∞¶‡∞æ‡∞∞‡∞ø WAY OUT Mahadvara (Inside wall) charya: “The door jambs, the sills and soffits of these are wide and thick cut stone and carved in the traditional style. The front door-way alone has folding doors, hung on pivots and made of stout lattice wood work, backed with thick battens fastened together by stout iron nails having artistic heads. There is also at trap door on the left side door to be kept closed” (History of Tirupati, Vol I, TTD, Tirupati 1953 p.167). An interesting detail found in the outer prakara-wall of the temple was pointed to out to me during a recent visit of mine to the hill-shrine by my friend, Shri D.V. Chary, who was then the Executive Engineer of the TTD. This pertains to the ‘writing on the wall’ (in the literal and literal and not the metaphorical sense), which provides a clue to the manner in which the stones were put up. The familiar sacred formula used in several rituals, “Om namah sivƒÅya, siddham namah” has been employed here to mark the stones for vertical and horizontal placements. The first letter ‘om ‘is given to the uppermost layer, the second letter ’na’ to the second layer below, and so on. When the ten letters of the formula are used up thus, then the letters of the alphabet ‘a’, ‘a’, T, T and so on are taken up to mark the rows of stones. To indicate the sides of the prakara-wall, the letters ‘da’ (for dakshina, south), ‘u’ (uttara, north) ’tu’ (turpu, east) and ‘pa’ (paschima, west) are used to mark the stones. The height of each layer of stones is from thirteen to fifteen inches. The drawing given here represents this coding on the southern prƒÅkƒÅra-wall. The presentday pilgrim will enter this doorway which is surmounted by a tower or gopuram which rises to a height of about fifty feet above ground level; and here are compound-walls (about four feet thick), proceeding from the sides of this doorway, 414 feet east to west and 263 feet south to north, enclosing the shrine-area covering about two acres. The surrounding walls give the appearance of a fort rather than a temple. And the tower over the gateway, which stands on a The Hill Shrine 91 Height of the Wall ‡∞ì -‡∞¶-‡±ß |

The surrounding walls (outer prakƒÅra) may date only from about 1260 A.D.; but we do not find mention of their presence till 1320 or 1330 A.D. The processional image of the Sri-rangam shrine was housed in the mantapam inside this prakaram in 1320 or 1330. The sanctum of this mantapam almost abuts the southern prakƒÅra-wall. The earliest inscriptions on this outer prakara are all of the fifteenth century. It is also suggested that the inner prakara-wall which encloses the Vimana-pradakshina was raised earlier than this outer compound-wall, for the inscriptions on the former wall belong to an earlier period. As T.K.T. Viraraghavacharya rightly notes: “It is thus seen that the second or outer prakaram wall inscriptions long after the first came into use for first one had been largely used” (op. cit., p.170). Our text (verse 55) mentions ‘mahƒÅdvara’, but does not refer to any gopura on it - or to the prƒÅkƒÅra-wall. It is likely that the ‘maha-dvara’ was only a tall gateway, an arch-sort of structure, without any superstructure above it, or walls emanating from its sides. Further, the text (same verse) says that the pilgrim would see a huge tamarind tree as soon as he enters through the gateway (‘sriman-mahƒÅddvaram pravisyantah asthana-chifichƒÅm achala-chhƒÅyƒÅm’). We do not, however, see it now. The text also mentions that there were a few mantapas (verse 65), probably free structures standing apart from each other, outside the inner enclosure. Thus, for about 120 feet from the outer gateway to the inner, there must have been open ground with trees alround the shrine, extending on the north-eastern side to the sacred tank (Svami-pushkari≈Üi). The puranas associate the tamarind tree with the manifestation of the hill-god. The Vaikhanasa priest Gopinatha and his sudra attendant Ranga-dasa together recovered the image of the god from the huge anthill that was beneath the tamarind tree on the banks of Svami-pushkari≈Üi and installed it on the western side of the tree. (Brahma-purana 7, 30) ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§ø‡§µ‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§≤‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§π‡§∞‡•á‡§∞‡§ø‡§¶‡§Æ‡•ç The Hill Shrine 8 93 ‡§â‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§Æ‡•á ‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§™‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§∏‡§§‡•Å‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§§‡•å ‡•§ This agrees with the present location of the god in the sanctum and the purana specifies that the image was hidden under the ant-hill only till the knees (jƒÅnu-mƒÅtra nimagna hi-tintrini vrksha-mulatah’, 20-21) According to Varaha-purana (9, 1650), the two of them (Gopinatha and Rangadasa) discovered the image of the god, freely standing (‘akasastha’) beneath the tamarind tree on the southern bank of Svami-pushkari≈Üi, the image was exposed to the sun, moon, wind and rain. They levelled the ground and cleared it of all vegetation, barring the tamarind tree under which the god’s image was found and a champaka tree which was dear to Lakshmi. The tamarind tree apparently stood near the outer gateway while the champaka tree was near the present inner gateway. The circumambulation known as Sampangi-pradakshina (sambaga-chchurru) is called so because of the presence of the champaka tree, left uncut by the two builders of the original shrine. Bhavishyottara-purana (4, 19-21) explains the hidden meaning of the ant-hill, the tamarind tree, the sacred tank and the hill itself. The hill-god is a reincarnation of Krishna of the Dv para age. His mother Devaki now too’ the shape of an ant-hill, and his father Vasudeva appeared in the form of the tamarind tree; his brother Bala-bhadra (who is an incarnation of Sesha or Ananta) was the hill, Seshadri; the river Yamuna became Svami-pushkari≈Üi and the land around it was Mathura indeed. All the animals on this hill are in fact the Yadavas who were playmates of Krshna, and the birds are none other than the gopika-damsels. ‡§µ‡§≤‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•Å‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•ã ‡§§‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä ‡•§ ‡§¨‡§≤‡§≠‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§É ‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡§∂‡•à‡§≤‡•ã ‡§Æ‡§•‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§¶‡§ß‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡§æ‡•§‡•§ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•Ä ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§æ ‡§ö ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§§‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§µ‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§Æ‡•É‡§ó‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•á ‡§ñ‡§ó‡§æ ‡§µ‡•à ‡§ó‡•ã‡§™‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§É‡•§ ‡§è‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§ï‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§£‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡•á‡§£ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§°‡§§‡•á ‡§µ‡•á‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ü‡§æ‡§ö‡§≤‡•á‡•§‡•§ PRATIMA-MANTAPA At present, one who enters through the first gateway (maha-dvƒÅra or mukha-dvƒÅra) finds himself within an open mantapa known sometimes as Pratima-mantapa, for the splendid portrait icons (pratima) kept here. This mantapa is continuous with the gateway structurally. It is rectangular in shape, about 27 feet in width and 24’ 6” in length. Leaving a passage of 16’ feet in the middle, the mantapa has two wings, the southern one being 10'6" wide and the northern one 9'6". The wings have pillars, and the entire mantapa rests on sixteen pillars. As you enter facing the shrine, to your right is a group of three copper cast statues of excellent workmanship. 94 The Hill Shrine Pratima-Mantapa (view from inside) 95 They represent the famous Vijayanagar emperor Krishnadeva-raya (1509-1530) and his two consorts Tirumala-devi on his left and Chinna-devi to his right. The names are inscribed on the shoulders of the statues. They were set up by the emperor himself on 2nd January 1517. All three of them stand facing the shrine, with hands joined in supplication. This wing of the mantapa has two rows of four pillars (even as the other has). To your leit (viz. in the southern wing of this mantapa) is the copper-cast statue of another king of Vijayanagar, Venkatapati-raya of the Aravidu dynasty, who ruled over Chandra-giri around 1570, with the name name inscribed on the 96 Image of Venkatapati-raya king of Vijayanagar, in the Pratima-mantapa Image of Venkatapati-raya (close up) shoulder. By its side are the stone statues of Achayuta-raya, who succeeded Krishnadeva-raya on the throne and his wife VaradƒÅji-amma. But the identification of these stone statues is doubtful, there being no inscription of names on them. These images have been made perfectly in accordance with the Agamic prescriptions: “The figures of a devotee must be made realistically in the eight-tala measure, in gold, silver, copper or in stone. The devotee must hold his hands in the posture of supplication; his eyes must be settled on the tip of his nose; and his gaze must be one of abstraction. If he is a The Hill Shrine Image of Krishnadeva-rƒÅya with queens (in Pratima-mantapa) The Hill Shrine 97 Image of Achyutaraya-maharaya, king of Vijayanagar, in the Pratima-Mantapa, with his queen kshatriya, he must wear the crown on his head, but he must be shown in the attire of a devotee. He must be impressive -looking, and his limbs must be well-proportioned (SriVaikhanasa Pratishthanukramanika, Part II 36th chap. ed. Divi Venkata-n·πõsimha charya, Nallur, 1978, pp. 300-301). foll ‡§Ö‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§§‡§æ‡§≤‡•á‡§® ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Å ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡•ç || ‡•¶ || ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§≤‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§ï‡•Å‡§ü‡•ã‡§™‡•á‡§§ .. ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§≠‡§∞‡§£‡§∏‡§Ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡§Æ‡§•‡§æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§µ‡§æ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ‡§∏‡•å‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§Ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§µ‡§Ø‡§µ‡§∂‡•ã‡§≠‡§ø‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡§ø ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§¶‡§æ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡§æ‡•§‡•§ Pratima-Mantapa (Pillars) The text does not mention this open mantapa or any other mantapa specifically; it merely says ‘mantapan’. Then it takes the pilgrim stranghtaway to the Dhvaja-stambha-mantapa, where the bali-pitha also is located. From there, the pilgrim is supposed to go round the enclosure and come back to the Dhvaja-stambha-mantapa; and enter into the first Gateway surmounted by a tower, and then come to Vimana pradakshina. From 1987, the scales for the tulabhara offerings by the devotees (weighing themselves against silver, gold, jaggery etc), were placed in this mantapa. Till then they were located 98 The Hill Shrine near the Koppara (Hundi) in the TirumƒÅmani-mantapa. TWO PRAKARAS In terms of the present-day temple plan, there are Two enclosures (prakara-walls) for the shrine. Between two prakara walls is the circumbalatory path, pradakshina-patha. Between the outermost prakara wall and inner prakara-wall is the pathway for circumambulation known as Sampangi-pradakshina. And between the inner prƒÅkƒÅra-wall and the shrine proper (consisting of the sanctum, Sayana-mantapa, RƒÅmar-mƒìda, Snapana-mantapa and Tirumamanimantapa)is the pathway known Vimana pradakshina, for the golden Vimana (Ananda-nilaya) is visible for the pilgrims as they go round. There closed is another pathway which is for the pilgrims except for a single day in a year, between the prakƒÅra wall of the five limbed shrine and the wall round the sanctum, the Sayana-mantapa, and Raman-meda. This narrow and covered pathway is known as Vaikuntha-pradakshina or mukkotipradakshina, on account of its being kept open on Vaikuntha or Mukkoti ekƒÅdasi. All Two pathways have their openings only on the eastern side. The Hill Shrine Mahadwara (Inside: north-face basement) 99SAMPANGI-PRADAKSHINA Of these three, the middle pathway (viz. Sampangi-pradakshina) contains several interesting mantapas like Ranga-mantapa, Tirumala-raya-man- tapa, would be ‘antar-mandala’; for the second (or middle) path (viz. Vimanapradakshina), ‘madhya-hara’; and for the third (viz. Sampangi-pradakshina) ‘maha-maryadi’. However, the mahapradakshina (viz., going round the four counted here. not streets around the shrine) is Saluva-Narasimhamantapas, Dhvaja-stambha-ma·πá·π≠apa and Ainamahal. In fact, the so-called Pratimamantapa which we have just now described is also in this pradakshina. At present, however, no pilgrim can use this pathway for circumambulation. The queue lines as well as the irregular (even haphzard) contours of the mantapas make it difficult even to visualize the pathway. In terms of temple-structure, the agama-texts prescribe from one to seven enclosure-walls (prƒÅkƒÅra). The path between two prakara-walls is meant not only for circumambulation but the pavilions abutting the outer wall of this path (called madil-mantapa) would accommodate several requirements and functions of the temple. In the case of the hill-shrine there are three prƒÅkƒÅrawalls, and three circumambulatory paths. The innermost path now called Vaikuntha or Mukko»õipradakshina is bound on the outer side by the wall that becomes the inner wall of the middle one, now called Vimana-pradakshina. The outer wall of this path will become the inner wall of Sampangi-pradakshina, the outer wall of which is also the outer compound wall of the entire temple. The agama terminology for the first (or innermost) circumambulatory path 100 In former times, this pathway was an open one, not enclosed by any prƒÅkƒÅra-wall. It is also said that there were champaka trees around this pathway, which circumstance was responsible for its name (ChampakƒÅvara·πáa, Sampangi-pradakshina, Sambaga-churru). The mantapas that were there were probably stray and unconnected structures. As mentioned earlier, one could, according to this text (verse 66), see the sacred tank, Svami-pushkarini, without a hindrance from the Dhvajastambha-mantapa. And the shrine at the far end of the present Rangamantapa does not have the outer prƒÅkara-wall as its back on the southern side; there is another mantapa (used formerly as a sort of kitchen and as Panel-Room, security room and queue line) between the outer prakara-wall and the Ranga-mantapa. This suggests that the outer prakarawall was a later construction; originally the Ranga-mantapa must have been an isolated structure the open ground, at a distance from the present Dhvaja-stambha-mantapa (which must have been there even when this Ranga-mantapa was raised). now on The nathway known as Sampangipradakshina skirted close to the inner The Hill Shrine prƒÅkƒÅra-wall (viz., outer prƒÅkƒÅra wall of the Vimana-pradakshina), which appears to have been built around 1190 A.D. There was no other wall enclosing this pradakshina at the time our text was prepared. The Bali-pitha and the Dhvaja-stambha were outside the precincts of the shrine. now a In the north-western corner of this pradakshina there is even spring-well, small and shallow, called Viraja-nadi; at present its water is unfit for use. But it must have been in use in former days. Saluva-Narasimha constructed around 1470 A.D. four mantapas in the open court-yard at the four corners of Sampangi-pradakshina, in the names of himself, his wife and his two sons. On occasions of festival, the processional image of the hill-god was brought out to go round this pradakshina, and it held court in each of these mantapas, when food-offerings were made to the pilgrims. Each mantapa is tall, unenclosed, and stands on four plain pillars. The four mantapas, however, are not of the same size. The two at the corners of the Sampangi-pradakshina behind the shrine are 8’ 6” square each, while the one at the south-eastern corner is 11 feet square and the other at the north-eastern corner is 11’ 6" x 12’ 3" When, however, the outer prakƒÅram wall was raised, mantapas or rooms were constructed along the wall in The Hill Shrine the front of the long corridors on the three sides (excluding the eastern) till the Dhvaja-stambha-mantapa. Beyond the Dhvaja-stambha-mantapa, were large structures like the Tirumala-raya-mantapa and Ranga-mantapa on southern side. The rooms constructed in front of the Sampangi-pradakshina were used as Ugrana (store-room), Pa·π≠·π≠ƒÅdi (the room where silk garments were preserved), RƒÅmƒÅnuja-k≈´tam (where pilgrims were fed freely, constructed in 1450), Padi-potu ( kitchen for the preparation of sweets known as ‘panyƒÅrams’, fried and baked delicacies and Yamunat-turai (where flower-garlands for the god were strewn) A word may be said here about Yamunat-turai, where flower-garlands for daily worship are prepared. It is a custom of great antiquity in this temple that specific numbers of garlands are prepared each day, and each of them of a specific length. The length of the garlands is in terms of ‘mura’ (18 inches) and ‘bara’ (36 inches). The list of garlands used in tomala-seva everyday is given below:

In 1923, some rooms were built on the southern side to store several articles relating to teppotsavam (floatfestival). Now these rooms are used to store la·∏çdus. The small tub-like watersource on the north-eastern corner of the shrine has been used to deposit the used flowers in worship (nirmalya) every day. The custom in the hillshrine forbids flowers worn by the god being distributed to the devotees; such flowers are dumped in this tub called pula-bavi (’the well for flowers’). In 1952, this tub was converted to a small well. The Paimayishi Account mentions that on the northern side of Sampangi-pradakshina there was a Suvarnamantapa of four storeys. Our text (verse 67) also mentions a Suvarnamantapa, which the pilgrim is said to see in this pradakshina, after beholding SvƒÅmi-pushkari≈Üi. It is difficult to determine what this Suvarna-mantapa refers to. The text also mentions that the pilgrim would see NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa-giri after seeing Suvarna-mantapa (‘suvarnamantapam chaiva narayana-girim tatha’). Does it mean that the custom was to climb the four-storeyed mantapa mentioned in the Paimayishi Account, and have a look at the highest peak (3620 feet) Narayana-giri from a disiance? NƒÅrƒÅya·πáa-giri peak is claimed to be the spot where the hill-god The Hill Shrine landed; there are the stone imprints of his feet on the peak, which the hardy pilgrims visit. According to our text, the order of noteworthy details in this part of the pilgrim’s journey are Padipotu (kitchen ), Svami-pushkarini (the sacred tank), Yamuneya-malyƒÅgƒÅra (the present Yamunat-turai), the icon of Krishna installed therein, Suvarna-mantapa, NarƒÅyana-giri and Bali-pitha. It has already been mentioned that the Puranas explain that the hill-god is a reincarnation of Krishna of the Dvapara age; the ant-hill being Devaki, the tamarind tree Vasudeva, the hill Bala-bhadra and SvƒÅmi-pushkari≈Üi the river YamunƒÅ. The former is called Ranga-nayakula-mantapa in the official Dittam for Kartikotsava (enumerating the spots in the temple-complex where lights have to be put up). It is also called Ranga-natha-mantapa, because it was constructed to house the processional image of the god Ranga-natha of Srirangam (which was being secretly conveyed from place to place to save it from Muslim vandalism during 13201360); and also because the Yadava- RANGA-MANTAPA (Inside) 107 RANGA-MANTAPA (Pillars) RANGA-MANTAPA (Northern corner) RANGA-MANTAPA (Western side) RANGA-MANTAPA (Hall) 108 The Hill Shrine 9 RANGA-MANTAPA (Sculptures) RANGA-MANTAPA (Inside) RANGA-MANTAPA (Inner mantapa) 109 The Hill ShrineT RANGA-MANTAPA (Central Hall) RANGA-MANTAPA (Vijayanagar royal emblem on the entrance pillars) RANGA-MANTAPA (Ramayana-frieze) 110 The Hill Shrine RANGA-MANTAPA (Western wall) king, ≈öri-ranga-natha, was responsible for its construction. It would be more reasonable to assume that if this mantapa was constructed to be a shrine for the god of Sri-rangam who was worshipped according to the Pancharatra division of the Agama, it should be outside the premises of the hill-god, who was worshipped according to the VaikhƒÅnasa division of the Agama. During 1320-1360, therefore, the outer limit of the hill-shrine must have been the Bali-pitha, and the present Ranga-mantapa would have been built beyond this, in the open ground. And even now, it can be seen that this mantapa is a shrine in its own right. It has a sanctum with a vimanam of plaster over it, and a separate kitchen and rooms for torches and vahanas (cf. Paimayishi Account which states that at the southern side of the Sampangi-pradakshina in this mantapa consisting of a room for torches, a room for keeping Mysore state’s vehicles, Ranga-nayaka’s kitchen and a shrine). It must have been an unenclosed pavilion, large enough for the Sri-vaishnavas from Sri-rangam and Tirupati to gather for congregational worship during the many festivals that are celebrated in a Pancharatra shrine (unlike in a Vaikhanasa shrine) 112 The Hill Shrine This mantapa, supported by massive granite pillars, delicately sculpted in the Vijayanagar style, stands on a high basement, and measures 44’ 10" east to west and 112’ 7" north to south. At the far end on the southern side is the shrine (garbha-g·πõha and antarala) that was mentioned earlier, square in shape (12 feet) with a porch. It is called in the Paimayishi Account ‘Simhasana - mantapa’, having four slender posts in front of it; adjoining it on the west, continues the Account, is the rice-flowering room (pindi-salƒÅ). The doors of the shrine were closed and locked up, after the Pancharatra god was taken back to Sri-rangam, when favourable conditions prevailed there. A large Sesha-vahana is kept there now, blocking the entrance to this shrine. The hall was later used for functions like kalyƒÅnotsava. And the vahanas (processional vehicles) have been kept here now in the rectangular space to the west (15'2" wide) TIRUMALA-RAYA-MANTAPA Adjoining the Ranga-mantapa on the western side, farther removed from the southern prƒÅkƒÅra-wall (about 10 feet) and facing the Dhvaja-stambhamantapa, is a spacious complex of The Hill Shrine RANGA-MANTAPA (Left) and Tirumala-Raya-Mantapa 113 114 TIRUMALA-RAYA-MANTAPA The Hill Shrine TIRUMALA-RAYA-MANTAPA (basement of the northwestern corner) TIRUMALA-RAYA-MANTAPA (Front View) The Hill Shrine pavilions (38 feet east to west and 77 feet north to south) generally known as Tirumala-raya-mantapa, after the chieftain Tirumala-raja of the Araviti family. Continuous with this inner and earlier mantapa (called in inscriptions Narasimha-raya-mantapa, II, 79) which is rectangular in shape, is another mantapa, lower in level and reached by steps, which faces Dhvaja-stambhamantapa. This latter mantapa is almost square in shape (35’) and at its north-western corner is the platform (13’ long) on which the portrait statues of T√∂·∏çar-mal are kept. The distance between this mantapa and the prakƒÅrawall by the side of the Inner Gate-way (Na·∏çimi-padikavali) is 17 feet; and the distance between this mantapa and the Dhvaja-stambha-mantapa is 10 feet. was This mantapa was built by Araviti Tirumala-raya for the performance of the annual Vasantosava. He also arranged for the free distribution of food -offerings (made to god )to the pilgrims; this well-known as ‘Tirumala-rayan pongal’. Latterly, the custom came into vogue of seating the processional image (Malayappa) in this pavilion to hold court on the evening of the flag hoisting ceremony (dhvajaroha·πáa), marking the commencement of the grand Brahmotsavam. The location of this mantapa facilitated this practice. Of the three statues the one of Pita-bibi (wife) is complete, that of Lala-Khem-RƒÅm The Hill Shrine was a AINA-MAHAL Among the other structures within the Sampangi-pradakshina is an usual Hall of Mirrors (Aina-mahal, or Addala-mantapam), built on a high basement, to our right as we enter through the Outer Gateway. It faces the Ranga-mantapa on the other side and is about 12 feet away from Pratimamantapa. This is obviously a late addition to the temple-complex. The open front pavilion is square in shape (43’ 13" x 43’ 6") and there are six rows of six pillars in each row. There is an enclosed room of about the same size (41’ 9" x 42’) behind this open pavilion, with its entrance to the east. Inside this room, there is a sanctum-sort of apartment (23’ x 2’ 9") with a pathway alround it. The walls of the room are faced with large mirrors. There is also a railing in the middle of the room. It is not known why or when this room was built. For a long time, the administrative offices of the temple were located here. Now, the laddus are stored in this hall. In effect, the pathway freely available in Sampangi-pradakshina is the open space actually around the outer wall of Vimana-pradakshina. This path- 121 www DHVAJA-STAMBHA-MANTAPA (View as we enter through the Main Doorway) way is 30’ 7" wide on the southern side, 21 feet behind the shrine. I remember that as an youngster, I would, as would all the other pilgrims, go up to the Dhvaja-stambha-man·π≠apa and turn left to enter into the Sampangi-pradakshina. The eastern bit of this pathway towards south was very narrow, because of the Tirumala-rayanmantapa coming very close, whereas the other side of the eastern prƒÅkƒÅra (towards north) was much far removed. Now, of course, the Sampangi-pradakshina cannot be used by the pilgrims at all. The only pradakshina that is permitted by the TTD authorities at present is the Vimana-pradikshina. 122 DHVAJA-STAMBHA-MANTAPA (View along with Nadimi-Padikavall and Ananda-nilaya). vimana) DHVAJA-STAMBHA-MANTAPA The Dhvaja-stambha-mantapa, where the pilgrim would find the Bali-pitha (seat for food offering) and the Dhvaja-stambha (flag-pole), is located physically in the Sampangipradakshina, but technically it is the outer limit of the Vimana-pradakshina. According to the Agama requirements, bali-pitha and dhvaja-stambha must be found in the first enclosure itself (prathamavarana). But to have a pavilion (mantapa) for the Bali-pitha and Dhvaja-stambha is rather unusual. Being a hill-shrine, the uncertainties of the weather conditions probably necessitated a covered mantapa, where the The Hill Shrine DHVAJA-STAMBHA-MANTAPA (northern side) rituals may be carried out even when it rains, for the very purpose of a dhvaja-stambha and bali-pitha is to facilitate festivals. It is said in Bh·πõgu’s Khiladhikara (32, 19) ‡§π‡§∞‡•á‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§§‡•ã ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§¨‡§≤‡§ø‡§™‡•Ä‡§†‡§Ç ‡§ß‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡•§ ‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•á‡§® ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡•å ‡§π‡•Ä‡§®‡§Ø‡•ã‡§∞‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É‡•§ The relative positions of the two details are in accordance with Agamic prescriptions. The flag-pole must be, according to the texts, between the dvara-gopura (gateway-tower) and the bali-pitha (altar), directly in front of the entrance, and the altar is situated immediately behind it.
