The the flag-pole and altar together measure 15 feet east to west. In the space between the Bali-pitha and the Dhvaja-stambha are kept five small altar-stones; it is on these that the bali (food offerings for the attendant divinities) is actually placed. The pavilion that contains the flagpole and the altar is a structure 36 feet square, and it stands on twenty stone pillars, on which are carved several erotic figures and also some incarnations of Vishnu (like Narasimha, Matsya and Krishna). There is also to be seen the Pandya emblem of a pair of fish (matsya-yugma). The flag-pole and the altar were renewed during the renovations that were carried out in 1982. THE INNER ENTRANCE ‡§¨‡§≤‡§ø‡§™‡•Ä‡§†‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ó‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§ö‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§¨‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡§É ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∏‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡•Å‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ||‡•¨‡•Æ|| the The pilgrim now returns to the Balipitha from where he began his circumambulation along Sampangi-pradakshina. He enters through the gateway that leads him to Vimana-pradakshina. The towered 128 gateway is known as Nadimi-padikavali, or middle main entrance. The temple has only two towered gateways (dvara-gopuras); the outer entrance which admits the pilgrim into the temple -complex (Padi-kavali or Mahadvara) pierced through the outermost enclosure, and the inner entrance, the wooden doors of which are covered by silver plates (hence known as Vendi-Vakili). The distance between the two gateways is about 120 feet, covering an open courtyard. The inner gateway is reached only through the Dhvaja-stambha-mantapa. The two gopuras are similar in design, but the inner one is smaller, its height being nine feet and base 24 feet east to west and 36 feet south to north; and the width of the gateway is 9’ 6". It has three tiers (talas) of plaster work, surmounted by ornamental vases. The enclosure. walls that emanate from the sides of this gateway measure on the outside 235 feet east to west and 160 feet south to north. The walls are faced with dressed cutstones, about three feet thick. There are numerous inscriptions to be found here, the earliest relating to the Pandyan monarch, Jata-varma Sundarapandya, who began his rule in 1251 A.D. This enclosure, thus, was in existence long before the outer enclosure took shape. This gopura like the outer one, was renovated between 1472 and 1482 (cf II 79) and again in 1950 - 53. ‡§™‡§æ‡§Ø‡§∏‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§°‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡•à‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§∂‡•Å‡§ß‡•ç‡§¶‡•à‡§∞‡•ã‡§¶‡§®‡§∞‡§æ‡§∂‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É The Hill Shrine ‡§¶‡§ß‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Ä‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡•à ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡•ã‡§¶‡§®‡•à‡§É ‡••‡•¨‡•Ø‡•• ‡§Ö‡§™‡•Ç‡§™‡•à‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡•Å‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à: ‡§∂‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§™‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•ã‡§™‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•à‡§É ‡•§ ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ó‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡•å‡§ò‡•É‡§§‡•à‡§É ‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§ï‡•à‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§ó‡•Å‡§°‡§∏‡§Ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•à‡§É 110011 ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡•Å‡§ï‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§°‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§É ‡§∏‡§ú‡•Ä‡§∞‡§ï‡§Æ‡§∞‡•Ä‡§ö‡§ï‡•à‡§É ‡•§ ‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§≤‡§´‡§≤‡•à‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡•à‡§µ ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§®‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡•à‡§∞‡§™‡§ø||‡•≠‡•ß|| ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§æ‡§ï‡§∂‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§• ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§§‡§É ‡•§ ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§®:‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ||‡•≠‡•® || ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç……‡•§ Pattika 1 Kantha ICICICI Padma-jagati The Hill Shrine Upa-pitha of the Nadimi-padi-kƒÅvali (Inner Entrance) Upana 129130 GARUDA VIMANA - PRADAKSHINAM g-t 12’-0" 12-0"‚Üí‚Üí‚Üí‚Üí Z SILVER DOORWAY Z DHVAJA-STAMBHA-MANTAPA NADIMI-PA√áIKƒÄVALI The Hill Shrine NADIMI-PADI-KƒÄVALI (basement on the northern side) NADIMI-PADI-KAVALI (Wall on the western side) NADIMI-PADI-KAVALI (Outside the prokora wall on the northern side) The Hill Shrine 131 VIMƒÄNA-PRADAKSHINA The pilgrim who passes through the inner gopuram steps into the Vimanapradakshina, which is the circumambulatory pathway round the central shrine in the direct sight of the gilt dome over the sanctum (called Ananda-nilaya-vimƒÅna). The use of this name for the enclosure appears to have begun in 1190 A.D. Besides the open ground for devotees to go round, there are also many apartments which are significant in the history and administration of the temple. Our text deals with only a few of them; it will, therefore, be desirable to give a complete picture of the Vimana-pradakshina as it obtains now. As soon as we enter the Vimanapradakshina, we see the back of the Garudalvar-sannidhi which blocks the main entrance to the open pavilion known as TirumƒÅma≈Üi-mantapa. The pavilion is removed from the Inner Gateway by about 30 feet. The space available for circumambulation, or rolling around on ones body in fulfilment of a vow called anga-pradakshina, is 30’ 7" wide on the right side of the main shrine, till we reach the KalyƒÅ·πáama·πá·π≠apa (which intrudes into the passage by about 15’), 15 feet at the back of the shrine, and 21 feet on the left side. The Pradakshina is rectangular in shape, with 235 feet long (east to west) and 160 feet wide (north to south). The expression ‘vimana’ signifies in the Agama texts the main shrine itself or the dome (or (or tower) raised over the sanctum Maya-mata (22,8), Kamik√§gama (50, 91-93), Agni-purƒÅ·πáa (104, 14-15), and other texts use the word Vimana in the former sense, which apears to be the earlier usage. In the Vaikhanasa texts also, vimana refers to a temple, as for instance, in the well-known work of Marichi, VimƒÅnarchana-kalpa (7th patala). ‚Ä¢ Bhrgu’s Khiladhikara deals with the thirty-two kinds of temples under the section ‘vimana-bhedah’ (ch. 6); and uses the expression the vimana sometimes in the sense of a sanctum, as for instance (2, 77), ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§ß‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡•Ä‡§Ø ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§É‡•§ ‡§ß‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§ï‡•å‡§§‡•Å‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡•á‡§¶‡•ç ‡§¨‡•Å‡§ß‡§É ‡•§ or (20, 3-4), ‡§è‡§ï‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•á ‡§µ‡•à ‡§¨‡•á‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§£‡§ø ‡§ï‡§≤‡•ç‡§™‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§∂‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ã ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•á ‡§§‡§•‡•à‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ‡§∂‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§≤‡•ç‡§™‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ Kasyapiya J√±ana-ka·πá·∏ça J√±ana-kanda means by vimƒÅna a temple when it deals with the eighteen kinds of vimana (chap-32, ‘ashtadasa-vimanani devadevasya vishnoh’). In Yajnadhikara (ch. 5, 21) we have the same meaning in another context: ‡§∂‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§≠‡•á‡§¶ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§É‡•§ 132 The Hill Shrine NADIMI-PADI-KƒÄVALI (Wall on the western side) Likewise, in the puranas, vimƒÅna stands for a temple. In Bhavishyottara, for instance, the prince Samkha is advised by God himself to construct a temple (vimana) for him: ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§∂‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§≤‡•ã‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ñ‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡•§ (3,47) ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§§‡•á ‡§Æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§≠‡§∞‡§§‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡§≠‡•§ (3, 49) ‡§§‡•á‡§®‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡•á‡§£ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§™‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§™‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∂‡§Ç‡§ñ‡•ã ‡§®‡§∞‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ò‡•á The Hill Shrine ‡§ú‡§ó‡§æ‡§Æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§£‡•ã‡§É ‡§™‡§¶‡§Æ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•Å‡§≠‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§‡•§ (3, 51) In the Pancharatra reckoning, however, the vimana generally means the superstructure over the sanctum. Naradiya-samhita, for instance, speaks of decorating the vimana (viz. tower) with the figures of ten incarnations of Vishnu (13,307, ‘vimana-sobhartham’), or of different types of such superstructure (14, 111 rectangular, square, circular etc.), or of the rites of propitiations when a vimana gets broken (25, 287, ‘bhagne vimane’). The shrine itself is called in the Pancharatra texts ‘alaya’ or ‘prasada’. 133 134 Adhishthana of the Nadimi-padikavall (Inner entrance) Pattika Kantha Kumuda Jagathi Upana The Hill Shrine The Hill Shrine Entrance to Vimana-pradakshina 135 POTU —Ä–æ—Ç–∏ AMMA YAGASALA BANGARU-BAVI VIMANA-PRADAKSHINA VARADARƒÄJA SANNIDHI < 641-0-> 136 Outer wall of the sanctum in the Kalyana-mantapa in Vimana-pradakshina The Hill Shrine E VYALA PRASTARA UTTARA KAPOTA STAMBHA VEDIKA KAPOTA-PATT PADA-VARGA KUMUDA PADMA JAGATI ADHISHTHANA 00 ‚Üê6945"-> 4-61’-6-7 –æ—Å MUKKOTIPRADAKSHINA ‚Ä¢ 10.0 ‚Üê 8.3" 10’-d- ‚Ä¢ 4907 19 √•r ‚Üê 12-18‚Üí GARBHAN 5'9" 412-187 VIM√ÅNA-PRADAKSHINA BANGARU-BAVI +10.1'7 4967 ‚Üê 11-9, +13-67 125 ‚Üí The Hill Shrine ENTRANCE TO MUKKOTI-PRADAKSHINA 4170"> “416”-3" 10" 13'2" SAYANA 4-18 1 -> 411361‚Üí RAMAR 37 5.9 5'9" -27'6" ‚Üê27’ SNA PANA 3.4’ VISHVAXSNA STORE STORE ROOM ROOM TIRU MAMANI - MANTAPA 20’-0" ‚Üê 12'2"> ¬± 13'6 ->" Inside Vimana-pradakshina 4 30’ 0" <92-6-7 EXIT FROM < MUKKOTI- PRADAKSHINA 137 Vimana-pradakshina in a VaikhƒÅnasa shrine (like the hill-shrine) is actually the circumambulatory path that skirts the sanctum along with the pavilion in front of it, which is the main shrine. In this case, this is what is now the covered and narrow pathway called Mukkoti-pradakshina, which goes round the walls of the sanctum (garbha-g·πõha) and the present Sayana-mantapa, which in former days was only the mukha-mantapa (or the ’llan-k≈çyil’). It is not known when the wall that is at present called Vimana-prƒÅkƒÅra, and which is also closed for the devotees, was built. This is not mentioned in our text; nor does it suggest the presence of another (inner) pradakshina, besides Vimana-pradakshina. According to the text, the pilgrim goes round the shrine in Vimana-pradakshina and enters the main shrine directly. The text mentions most of the other details in this pradakshina, like the kitchen (paka-sala, p≈çtu), the sacrificial hall (yaga-salƒÅ), the shrine to Ramanuja, the shrine to Narasimha and also the Pushpa-mantapa (the present Tirumamani-mantapa). We have an inscriptional record that the last mentioned mantapa (which our text calls Pushpamantapa, verse 79) was built in 1417 A.D. So, till at least that date, there was no Mukkoti-pradakshina; Vimanapradakshina was a wide pathway, the inner side of which touched the walls of the sanctum and and the present Sayana-man·π≠apa. The small shrine to Vishvaksena, the existence of which is unknown to most of the pilgrims, but which is mentioned in our text, is situated in the present Mukkoti-pradakshina; and it faces east. There is a Vaikhanasa prescription that a separate shrine must be built for Vishvaksena in the very first enclosure (Khifadhikara 9, 7, 39) ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∏‡•á‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§ö‡•à‡§µ ‡§π‡§ø‡•§ ‡§Ü‡§≤‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä‡§§ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§•‡§Æ‡§æ‡§µ‡§∞‡§£‡•á ‡§™‡§ø ‡§π‡§ø ‡•§‡•§ and that the snapana-mantapa must be had in the second enclosure (ibid, 7, 64) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä‡§§ ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§∞‡§£‡•á ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡•§‡•§ The present Snapana-mandapa is a large apartment, larger in size than the sanctum, that stands in between the Ramar-Medai and Tirumamani-mantapa. Probably to satisfy the Agamic requirement to keep the snapana-mandapa outside the first enclosure, the inner circuit (Mukkoti-pradakshina) was constructed, including the shrine to Vishvaksena in it, as the Vaikhanasa agama requires. Thus, Mukkoti-pradakshina (more properly to be called Sannidhi-pradakshina) was carved out of the Vimana-pradakshina at some time after 1417 A.D. And because of the projecting shrine to Vishvaksena, on the northern side of the circuit, the width of Mukkoti-pradakshina on this side is almost double of what it is on the southern side. The Hill Shrine 138 VARADARƒÄJA-SHRINE is the When the pilgrim enters Vimanapradakshina and turns leftward, as is customary in the circumambulatory ritual, he beholds on the eastern side of the Vimana-pradakshina the small but elegant shrine to Varada-raja. This is not mentioned in our text. Varadaraja (Pon-mainda-perumal) presiding god at Kanchipuram (Attigiri), one of the most important places of worship for Sri-vaishnavas. We do not know why, or when, this little shrine to Varada-raja came to be here; there are no inscriptional or literary references to this. There is a conjecture that like the processional deity of Sri-rangam, the processional deity of Kanchipuram also sought and found refuge in the hill-shrine, when the Muslims threatened to attack this holy city. It is hard to ascertain the veracity of this conjecture. It appears that there is an inscription on the slab in front of the Tayar shrine in the Varadaraja temple (at Kanchipuram), according to which, “the Delhi Emperor Aurangazeb fitted out an expedition about 1688 A.D. against the Mahrattas of the South and Conjevaram. The temple authorities of the three premier temples of that city (viz., Varada-raja, Vaikuntha-perumal and Ulagalan·∏ça-perumal) thereupon apprehending desecration at the profane hands of the invaders, disguised the images of the temple gods as corpses and conveyed them secretly out of the The Hill Shrine in town and found an asylum in the jungles of U·∏çayar - palyam Trichinopoly district. But when the danger was past and Conjevaram was considered safe, the local chieftain of U·∏çayarpalyam, who was much enraptured at the image of the god, refused to restore it to its original abode at Kanchi with the result that at the special intereession of Attan-Jiyar, his disciple Lala-To·∏çarmalla terrorized the chief with a strong contingent of troops at his back and safely brought back in saka 1632 (1710 A.D.) the image and reinstated it in the temple with great pomp and splendour” (MED M-104-105 for 1920-21, cited in P.V. Jagadisa Ayyar South Indian Shrines, Madras 1922, pp 77-78) It becomes evident from this long extract that the hill-shrine is not mentioned here as the place where VaradarƒÅja was hidden, and that To·∏çaramalla, who was also a great devotee of the hill-shrine, as already mentioned, was also associated with the Varada-raja temple at Kanchipuram. For that matter, Achyuta-raya, the Vijayanagar king, was also a patron of the temple at Kanchipuram. There seems to have been thus a small shrine in the hill-temple even before 1354-55 A.D., as there is an inscription of this date on the northern wall of this shrine (in the 16th regnal year of Sri-ranga-natha Yadava-rƒÅya). However, that this small shrine was really to Varada-raja does not become evident by any inscriptional or literary 139140 NADIMI PADI-KAVAL VIMƒÄNA PRADAKSHINA 77Z/PRƒÄKƒÄRA-WALL /// W-24-0"> 12197 < «~11:0"> VARADARAJA-SANNIDHI The Hill Shrine | POTU
